Executive Summary
Ammonia Market Outlook Highlights: March 2015
Notable developments in the Q1 2015 Ammonia Market Outlook
•We have lowered our price forecast to around $400/t in 2015 and dropped it below $390/t in 2016 to reflect a more bearish outlook for key ammonia fundamentals. Prices of oil, contract gas, corn, DAP and urea are all falling as a response to deflationary energy markets and weak fertilizer demand growth and oversupply.
•A tighter ammonia market in 2015 is expected to provide shelter from the full force of collapsing fundamentals as production curtailments are expected to persist in Egypt, Ukraine and Trinidad. However, new merchant ammonia capacity coming on stream over forecast will add liquidity to the market and allow a closer alignment between ammonia and nitrogen values set by urea.
•Since the previous edition of this report we have added Yara/BASF 750,000t/y standalone, merchant ammonia plant to the forecast in 2019.
•Ammonia demand is expected to remain strong over the next five years, driven predominately by gains in urea, ammonium nitrate (including TAN) and DAP from 2017. However, a weak outlook for grain prices could limit growth.
A bear market ahead as ammonia loses fight with gravity

•However, CRU does not expect the ammonia market to be as exposed to deflationary energy markets as the urea market.
•The global supply/demand balance continues to look healthy and is not forecast to fall below 80% over our forecast period. An operating rate at this level should allow producers at the market’s margins to remain profitable.
•Ammonia also has a different cost profile to the urea market, and short-run marginal costs will be set by export orientated producers in Ukraine and Eastern on oil-linked gas contracts.

•All of the major fundamentals we track as indicators of ammonia price trend have undergone a substantial devaluation over the last six months, which will weigh heavily on ammonia values.
•This has forced us to reappraise our cyclical price forecasts and implement a significant downwards revision in 2015 and 2016.
•However, our view of the market for 2018 and 2019 remains little changed. And as can be seen in the chart opposite we are forecasting a substantial rebound in pricing fundamentals by 2017.
Data: CRU, IFA
• Brazil and Argentina are both in the midst of a record soyabean harvest and futures quotations have fallen sharply since December. This reflects a forecast 40% rise on global inventory levels. Meanwhile, corn futures prices have remained steady since finding a floor in September, with lower prices seemingly triggering extra demand.
• As soyabean prices move lower, and corn holds its ground, the soybean:corn price ratio has moved considerably, with the Soy Nov15 contract:Corn Dec 15 contract currently at 2.3:1, compared to 2.4:1 at the beginning of the year. With the spring planting period approaching, this should help to boost corn 2015 plantings and nitrogen demand.
Agricultural demand is forecast to support operating rates...
Global ammonia demand is forecast to reach 176.9 million tonnes in 2015, up from the 173.1 million estimated for 2014. With global grain and oilseed prices reaching a period of consolidation following consecutive ample grain and oilseed harvest, fertilizer demand over the coming years is likely to be pressured. This will particularly be the case for cost-conscious agricultural producers such as the United States.
There has been much industry conjecture in the USA over 2015 crop areas as we approach the spring planting season. Lower corn prices seen throughout 2014 are thought to have favoured soyabeans over corn for planned planting. CRU sees corn harvested area falling 1.9%, to 33.0 million hectares, with soyabean area expected to be 33.7 million hectares, similar to 2014. Lower corn area will pressure demand for ammonia, particularly direct application ammonia, a common source of nitrogen in the Midwestern states.
Since the beginning of 2015 the soyabean:corn price ratio has declined to levels supporting corn planting. While the ratio was supportive for more soyabean plantings in the key November-December 2014 period (when crop choice decisions are made), the steep declines in soyabean prices in 2015 has seen the ratio slip below a possible ‘crop switch point’. This may spur some extra corn plantings and ammonia demand, particularly in the Midwest where an early start to fieldwork is expected due to recent warm and dry conditions. It must be noted that the area available to crop switching in the Midwest is limited to around 3% of total arable area.
Phosphate fertilizers are the most sensitive to low agricultural prices and are often the first to be cut when farmers look to reduce input costs. If crop prices continue to move lower over 2015 and 2016 but fertilizer prices remain steady, both demand and production of ammoniated phosphates may be throttled. The lag period of such throttling must be considered. Declines in demand will cause a build up of inventories, which will later trigger cuts in production and therefore ammonia demand.
...and the floor price will support prices in 2015 as oil lags gas prices
Producers in Ukraine, as well as other major producing regions in Central and East European, rely on supplies of imported Russian gas as the feedstock for their ammonia production process. These gas supplies are sold on long-term take-or-pay contracts, indexed to the price of Brent crude oil on what are termed Groningen-type contracts. These contracts are based on replacement value theory: in the past oil was consumed in Europe for power-generation and heating purposes and was substitutable for gas. As Russian gas had/has very low production costs, it was priced on an oil basis to account for its substitutability for oil in the European energy consumption mix.
In the table we have used our calculated oil-indexed gas price for Ukraine (nine-month lag to our forecast of Brent crude) and modelled the weighted-average cost of ammonia exports at Yuzhnyy port. The costs are taken from CRU’s Nitrogen Fertilizer Cost Report and are on an annualised basis. This means it is an average breakeven price for the year and a producer may well, for a period, sell at a price lower than its annual breakeven price in the expectation that prices may recover in the future. There are also costs such as maintenance and payroll taxes that do not require in-year payment and may be postponed.
Ukraine: Export Costs, 2014-19 ($/t)
A new wave of Russian nitrogen investment is quietly growing...
In July 2013, Eurochem announced its intention to build a world scale ammonia and urea plant in Louisiana, citing a wish to establish production closer to its sizable US customer base, and capitalize on readily available energy, labour, infrastructure and logistics. However, global geopolitics has changed since then: both oil and the Russian rouble have lost almost half their value and sanctions have been placed on Russia over its involvement in Ukraine.
This has made Russian foreign investment less attractive for two reasons. Firstly, domestic production is cheaper for companies with international sales. This has certainly been the case for Eurochem which posted an EBITDA margin in Q4 2014 of 37%. The company estimates that with a USD/RUB close to 65 (as opposed to the 2013 average of 30), total operating cost savings in 2015 will be close to $500 million. Secondly, access to international capital markets has become more difficult. While Eurochem is technically now a Swiss company, the majority of its production assets (and large parts of its business) are based in Russia, and it will therefore be equally expensive for the company to service internationally (dollar) denominated interest on debt. Adding to these two factors are less favourable fundamentals in the USA where escalating capital costs and rapidly expanding capacity additions have been weakening the rational for further investment.
As the result of these factors we expect to see Russian nitrogen producers predominantly focus their investment efforts at home over the next few years, and not in other countries. At present there are four greenfield projects and one brownfield included in our committed forecast, with four more under consideration.

...While Yara and BASF commit to Freeport ammonia plant
The rush to build nitrogen fertilizer plants in the USA has added more than 5 million t/y of ammonia since 2009 but rapid escalation of construction costs has dampened activity in the last couple of years. In the chart below we have estimated how the rate of return on a 750,000 t/y ammonia plant in the US Gulf Coast depends on the initial capex and the price premium of its feedstock over Henry Hub. A plant like Dyno Nobel’s Waggaman, which cost $850 million to build and will buy its gas from Henry Hub, yields a 12.2% annual return – 2.4% higher than if it started being built today at a cost of $1 billion. Because of this, companies are seeking ways to reduce capital costs, even if it means paying a premium for their raw materials.
Capex ($m)
The latest innovative solution comes from Yara and BASF, which are building a 750,000t/y ammonia plant at BASF’s site in Freeport, Texas. Praxair has agreed a long-term contract to supply hydrogen, nitrogen and CO2 from its Texas City complex to be used as raw materials, saving an estimated $400 million by removing the requirement for a costly steam/methane reformer to produce hydrogen. We estimate an NPV of just $54 million for a conventional ammonia plant but $479 million if it were to cost $600 million instead of $1 billion. So in principle, Yara/BASF ought to accept to pay a premium over Henry Hub natural gas prices for its raw materials providing the NPV of the project remains above $54m. Given our stated assumptions, we believe that Yara/BASF could pay up to 49% more for its raw materials and still create a higher NPV than by building a standalone gas-based ammonia plant.
In practice, Yara/BASF will expect to negotiate a premium well below this maximum. However, Praxair is investing more than $400 million to add hydrogen and nitrogen capacity and extend its Gulf Coast pipeline network to Freeport. Notwithstanding the strategic benefits of connecting to the industrial zone in Freeport, Praxair will no doubt expect Yara/BASF to contribute to a return on this capital investment as well as covering its own costs of production. Furthermore, Praxair will recognize that Yara/BASF will be making a large capital saving that is wholly contingent on its arrangement with Praxair and hence expect a share of this saving through the pricing of its raw material supply contract. So although the bargaining zone for the two parties extends all the way from Praxair’s cost of production to a 49% premium over the cost of purchasing the equivalent quantities of natural gas from Henry Hub, we expect that the supply contract will be settled at a premium of perhaps 15-30%.
CRU has revised down Ammonia benchmarks in 2015 and 2016
We have downgraded our near-term ammonia forecast by around $75/t in response to a rapidly weakening outlook for ammonia market fundamentals. The urea price has collapsed at Black Sea and Middle Eastern FOB benchmarks as Chinese coal-based exports continue to oversupply the Pacific and Indian basins, while improved Ukrainian and North African output have increased availability East of Suez. Urea prices are not forecast to improve until 2017, in line with a recovery in global energy markets.
Although not as severe as in the urea market, oversupply is also weighing on DAP prices and reducing the operating rates at ammoniated phosphate plants. However, production is forecast to grow over the forecast period and will help maintain ammonia operating rates above 80% as urea rates decline. The corn market looks to have bottomed out and small gains are expected over the forecast. So while corn and DAP markets should support ammonia buying, the deflationary pressure from urea and energy markets will almost certainly outweigh any gains until they begin their recovery.

Chapter 1 - Demand
1.1 Global Ammonia demand overview
In 2014 ammonia demand is estimated to have reached 173.1 million product tonnes. CRU forecast this to increase to 176.9 million tonnes in 2015 and expand to 193.1 million tonnes by 2019. Urea production was responsible for 54% of total consumption in 2014 and is expected to remain the main component of demand over the medium term.
Table 1.1: Regional total ammonia demand
Diagram 1.1: Global ammonia demand components


0 20 40 60 80 100 120
Ammonia requirement (million product tonnes )
Other ammonia consumption includes NPKs, direct application and other nitrogenous fertilizers
Data: CRU
China is the dominant consumer of ammonia, responsible for 36% of total demand in 2014. This rate was higher than usual, reflecting a sharp increase in urea production in response to unprecedented export demand. Consumption of ammonia for technical uses is seen rising sharply in the medium term, offsetting projected declines in urea production. The United States remain the second highest user, followed by India, Russia and Indonesia. Growth in consumption over the medium term will be concentrated in Africa, Middle East, South-East Asia and South America. The Middle East and Africa will be driven by expansions in nitrogen facilities, with countries looking to monetise ample gas availabilities. Increased production of Caprolactam in China will support consumption in East Asia.

1.2 Agricultural Markets overview
Agricultural markets are important for ammonia demand despite ammonia not being directly applied to crops (except the United States). It is a key ingredient in nitrogen, phosphate and compound fertilizers. Consumption of these fertilizers is related to agricultural production and prices. The following section of this chapter outlines key market developments.
1.2.1 Recent agricultural developments
World grain production (wheat, corn, rice and other coarse grains) is estimated to have reached a record
2.5billion tonnes in 2014/15 (July/June), fractionally higher than the 2013/14 season. Global wheat output is seen at 725.0 million tonnes, up 1% compared to 2013 and a fresh high, with harvested area elevated 0.3% y-o-y. Outstanding yields were recorded in many countries, particularly within the EU and CIS. Output was lower in North America, with Canada returning to normal yields and a lower area following a near perfect 2013, while drought in the Southern Plains hampered winter wheat productivity in United States. A dry spring in south-eastern Australia forced a downgrade in production estimates, following an excellent start to the growing season. Output in Argentina grew 19% on good growing conditions and increased planted area.
2014/15 corn production is estimated at 991.3 million tonnes, a new record and up slightly from last year’s crop, despite a 1.6% decline in global harvested area. Record yields were reported in the United States and the EU, however, dry conditions in China’s northern growing area reduced output there. In Brazil, harvest of the first crop is progressing at a slower-than-average pace due planting delays caused by dryness. Safrinha (second) crop planting is now close to completion, with area forecast to be lower compared to last year (see section 1.3). Good rainfall and growing conditions in Argentina has been positive for crop prospects, with harvest in its early stages.
Soyabean output is set to reach a record 315.1 million tonnes in 2014/15, an 11% increase from the previous year, with United States production exceeding 100 million tonnes for the first time. Harvesting is underway in Brazil, with record production of 92 million tonnes expected, while favourable growing conditions in Argentina should see output expand to 57 million tonnes. Global harvested area is forecast to grow 4.2% y-o-y to 117.1 million hectares, underpinned by a 9.4% y-o-y increase in the United States.
Rice production is estimated to remain mostly unchanged from 2013. Minor gains in China, Indonesia, Bangladesh and Vietnam were not enough to offset a reduction in India’s output, reflecting a smaller summer (kharif) crop after delayed monsoon rains. Total output was seen at 475.5 million tonnes, down a modest 0.3% y-o-y, with harvested area increasing marginally to 162.4 million hectares.
Sugar output in 2014/15 is set for its second straight decline, falling 2% from the previous season to
172.5million tonnes. Brazil, the largest producer, is set to experience a 5% y-o-y reduction, to 35.8 million tonnes due to drought and continued low prices. Lower production in China, Thailand, Pakistan and Russia is seen offsetting gains in India, the EU and the USA. Global sugar inventories are considered to be in a ‘glut’, with prices at five year lows.
Consecutive years of strong grain and oilseed production have bolstered stocks to more comfortable levels, following tightness through 2012/13 which resulted in higher prices. These higher prices triggered increased planting and this, combined with favourable weather conditions, has resulted in significantly higher output and inventory levels.

With high levels of stocks, grain and oilseed quotations have fallen to their lowest since 2010. Although wheat prices found some support towards the end of 2014, they have slid significantly lower in 2015. Corn prices have remained steady over the past six months, but sharply down from levels seen over the past three years. We believe that prices have now found their floor which has triggered extra demand from end users, particularly within the livestock industry. Soyabean prices have seen a steady decline as Brazil output moves towards a fresh record and global stockpiles rise almost 40% year-on-year. Rice quotations also remain under pressure, despite a drop in stocks which have been steadily building over the past ten years. Abundant global supplies of grains and oilseeds should cap any major upward price movements in the near future, particularly in H1 of 2015.
Fertilizer prices have also seen steady declines since 2014, with strong export competition pressuring values. This has helped to ease the pressure on farmers as they face lower grain prices. While there were no significant fertilizer application cuts in 2014, CRU expect some light reductions in 2015, with phosphate fertilizers particularly sensitive the low crop prices. This could have a longer term impact on the ammonia demand for the production of DAP and MAP.
1.2.2 Short and medium term considerations
As the northern hemisphere comes out of winter and crops (mostly winter wheat) break their dormancy, a better judgement of crop prospects can be made. Crops in good condition are expected to consume more nitrogen, as they will have a greater yield potential, whereas crops in a poor condition are expected to see reductions in application rates. The table and diagram below give a brief assessment of winter wheat crop conditions in key growing areas, along with forecast harvested areas. In key urea consuming countries, conditions are generally considered as good, supporting demand for 2015.

As the northern hemisphere spring planting season approaches, the market can clearly focus on crop areas and prices and the respective impacts on fertilizer demand. Crop prices suffered significant losses in 2014, and have continued to move lower at the beginning of 2015. While this is not supportive for agricultural production, values still sit above long term levels. The diagram below shows current market sentiment. Traders have moved toward a net short position across the major agricultural commodities, suggesting further price falls in the near term. Lower crop prices are expected to impact planted area as farmers look save money on inputs where possible. Wheat harvested area is forecast to decline in the major producing countries. It should be noted that the EU-28 are the largest collective grower and exporter of wheat, with area there expected to grow 1% in 2015. Total wheat area in the Top 27 fertilizer consuming countries is seen falling for the first time since 2012. Corn area is seen moving lower in the US where growers are particularly price sensitive. Brazil area is also forecast lower due to a fall in safrinha plantings.
Despite high stocks and low prices, soyabean area is forecast to continue growing. Area in the US is tentatively projected to remain at 2014 levels, while Brazil plantings should continue to expand. Rice area is forecast fractionally higher, underpinned by improved plantings in India following last year’s monsoon
delays. Elsewhere, rice area is expected remain on trend, seen lower in China and slightly higher in other major Asian producers. These lower planted areas are expected to pressure total fertilizer demand. Although CRU expects a slight rebound in corn and wheat values in H2 of 2015, the consensus among other global forecasting agencies is for all prices to continue their downward trend. Longer term outlooks show that wheat and rice prices are expected to remain steady, while soyabean and corn values are projected to move higher, reflecting increased animal feed demand. Higher corn prices and stable wheat and rice levels will be supportive for fertilizer demand over the longer term. The recovery in soyabean prices later in the medium term will pressure nitrogen fertilizer demand, as soyabeans compete for land area with heavy nitrogen consuming crops like corn and spring wheat. However this will support phosphate demand.
Diagram 1.5: 2015 forecast harvested areas, top 5 growing countries
Data: CRU
The outlook for lower prices in the near term will pressure fertilizer demand, however this will be somewhat offset by softer fertilizer prices. Demand for both nitrogen and phosphate based fertilizers is expected to grow in 2015, underpinned by consumption in Asia. More developed markets, like North America and Europe may be pressured by lower crop prices, while growth in Brazil is set to continue, but at a lower rate than seen in previous years. Increased agricultural production and demand over the medium term will support fertilizer demand to 2019.
Nutrient Forecasting Methodology
CRU has developed a nutrient demand model, forecasting consumption at a country level over the next five years. Agricultural production is a function of food demand; therefore the first stage is to forecast global food demand, which is a function of population and GDP growth. Population growth determines total caloric requirements, while rising income affects food consumption (i.e. which food group will make up those calories).
The second and third stages are to forecast crop area and application rates by country. On a global basis, twenty seven countries (major consumers) account for approximately 90% of nutrient demand. By comparison, the remaining one hundred and fifty countries in our model (minor consumers), account for just 10% of global nutrient consumption. As such we have used two different approaches to calculate crop area and application rates in these two groups.
Concept of nutrient demand model
Growth in harvested area by crop in major consuming countries is based on our food demand projections. This is calculated using an historical series from the FAO, recent and current data from various sources, and forecasts from the CRU crop model. The forecast is then disaggregated by country and by crop, factoring in population, food intake, and average yields. All of these elements enable us to produce a robust forecast of production growth rates on a country and crop level, and thus a forecast of crop area calculated by country and by crop.
To calculate nutrient application rates by crop, we estimate the base year using IFA/FAO and FAPRI data or national sources. This is back-casted for all crops using country-level application rates, unless national sources are available, such as USDA data. The forecast is then based on recent trends and analyst judgement, and manual adjustments at country-level are made to reflect current apparent nutrient demand for N, P2O5 and K2O. A much simpler approach is used when calculating crop area and
application rates in minor consuming countries. In this case, we extrapolate recent trends to aggregate crop area by country and application rate by country. Thus, nutrient demand is calculated as a function of these.
Crop area forecast in major consuming countries
1.3 NH3 Demand from Ammoniated Phosphates
The most important developments in ammoniated phosphates supply through 2014 occurred in Africa, where widespread social unrest resulted in the curtailment of both phosphoric acid and concentrated phosphate fertilizer. This was most notable in Tunisia, where relentless strike action within GCT’s mining division (CPG) and logistics provider (Tunisian Company for the Transport of Mining Products in Gafsa), severely reduced rock supply. Phosphoric acid production and concentrated phosphate fertilizer production in Tunisia through 2014 fell to an estimated 731,000 tonnes P2O5 and 511,000 tonnes P2O5 respectively; a 21% and 29% reduction when compared to 2013. This resulted in a 40% decline in ammonia demand for DAP and MAP production, to 107,000 tonnes.
Additionally, North America saw reductions in production in 2014. In Q4, MissPhos idled phosphoric acid and DAP production, shortly after filing for Chapter 11 bankruptcy, with no clear intention of resuming operations. Also in Q4, Mosaic curtailed production in a successful attempt to pressure their suppliers of ammonia to reduce prices. Total United States demand for ammonia in MAP and DAP production fell 6% year-on-year, to 1.9 million tonnes.
Despite the production troubles outlined above and the fact that global capacity for concentrated phosphate fertilizer fell by 2% year-on-year, production increased by 3% to 31.1 million tonnes P2O5.
This increase in production was attributed to greater production in Asia, resulting from the successful commissioning of new plants there.
Global production of MAP increased to 12.1 million tonnes in 2014, up 1.8% from 2013 volumes. Production in CIS, North America and Africa declined but was outweighed by sharp gains in China. 2015 output is forecast to increase slightly, to 12.2 million tonnes, with demand for ammonia seen at 3.8 million tonnes. This modest growth rate is projected to continue over the medium term, with 2019 volumes seen at 12.4 million tonnes of MAP, with an ammonia requirement of 3.8 million tonnes.
DAP production experienced a sharper level of growth in 2014, moving 4.2% higher from 2013 volumes to 16.4 million tonnes P2O5. Output in China again underpinned growth, increasing 9.2% to 7.9 million tonnes. Volumes from Africa also increased but at a comparatively modest level, while the United States suffered steady losses. 2015 levels of DAP are forecast to remain steady, at 16.6 million tonnes, with an ammonia requirement of 8.3 million tonnes. This should accelerate over the medium term, to 18.6 million tonnes DAP by 2019 with an ammonia demand of 9.3 million tonnes.
Other regional developments expected to drive ammonia demand from ammoniated phosphate markets include:
•Downstream phosphate production capacity in Morocco is expected to increase significantly over the forecast period, with MAP capacity increasing from 2.1 million tonnes in 2014 to 3.5 million tonnes in 2019. DAP capacity will also increase over the outlook period, reaching 4.3 million tonnes by 2019, adding 1.3 million tonnes in 5 years. This translates into a total ammonia requirement of 1.1 million tonnes in 2019, a steep increase from the 0.8 million tonnes required for 2014. The shortfall in ammonia will be supplied by PotashCorp from Trinidad & Tobago. For this purpose, new ammonia storage capacity will be build at Jorf Lasfar, allowing enough storage of ammonia to sustain 45 days of ammoniated phosphate production at capacity.
•In Saudi Arabia, Ma’aden is expected to continue the expansion of current phosphate fertilizer capacity over the forecast period. MAP capacity will increase from current 0.5 million tonnes to
0.8million tonnes in 2019. In terms of DAP, capacity will increase sharply, from 3.3 million tonnes in 2014 to 5.8 million in 2019. This will translate into a doubling of the current ammonia requirement, to 1.2 million tonnes in 2019.
•Output in Brazil is also forecast to rise, with MAP capacity rising from 1.3 million tonnes in 2014, to 2.1 million in 2019, underpinned by strong domestic fertilizer demand as the agricultural industry continues its rapid growth. This will lead to an ammonium requirement of
0.3million tonnes by 2019, up from the current level of 0.2 million.
•The outlook for the ammonia demand from the phosphate sector in the United States is that of a decrease as US producers reduce MAP and DAP production in favour of NP+S over the forecast period, although capacity utilisation is expected to increase significantly following recent consolidations. Ammonia requirements will be reduced by more than 0.1 million tonnes, to 1.8 million tonnes by 2019. Ammonia is primarily imported from Trinidad & Tobago and the US state of Louisiana.
Diagram 1.7: Ammoniated phosphates production forecast

Data: CRU
Phosphate fertilizers are the most sensitive to low agricultural prices and are often the first to be cut when farmers look to reduce input costs. If crop prices continue to move lower over 2015 and 2016 but fertilizer prices remain steady, both demand and production of ammoniated phosphates may be throttled. However, the lag period of such throttling must be considered. Declines in demand will cause a build up of inventories, which will later trigger cuts in production and therefore ammonia demand. Global ammonia demand from MAP and DAP production in 2014 is estimated at 12.0 million tonnes. This is tentatively forecast to rise to 12.1 million tonnes in 2015 and expand to 13.1 million tonnes over the medium term. This will mainly be driven by expansions in DAP capacity, which is the phosphate fertilizer which is expected to grow the most and has the highest nitrogen content.
Table 1.5: Ammonia required in DAP and MAP (‘000 tonnes)
1.4 Anhydrous Ammonia (direct application) demand
The direct application of ammonia (anhydrous ammonia) in the US is a major source of crop nitrogen, with approximately 3.3 million tonnes (N based) applied each year. Its high concentration (82% N) means it is often the cheapest form of nitrogen available, although it must be stored under high pressure, requires specialised/expensive infrastructure and can only be applied when weather and soil conditions suit. Almost all global consumption of anhydrous ammonia occurs in the US.
The winter conditions have again been severely cold; however this is not thought to have had any major impact on crop or field conditions. Indeed, hard soil caused by freezing conditions in January allowed farmers in some states to progress with fertilizer applications prior to spring sowing. Conditions throughout February and March have not been advantageous for fieldwork, but snowfall on the corn-belt should boost soil moisture reserves. Current conditions in the Midwest have been warm and dry which may allow for an early start to fieldwork, encouraging additional ammonia demand.
Harvested areas for the major crops are likely to be lower in 2015, with plantings in marginal growing areas to be cut due to the lower crop price outlook. Wheat area is seen at 18.5 million hectares (18.8 million last year), with both winter and spring wheat areas expected to be lower. Spring wheat plantings will be pressured by low corn prices, as spring wheat is often used as a feed replacement for corn. The strong US dollar has reduced export demand for US wheat and is further expected to pressure prices. Corn harvested area is seen at 33.0 million hectares (33.6 million last year). Plantings in marginal areas are forecast lower; however with soyabean prices falling at a faster rate than corn, area may rise from initial expectations.
As farmers approach the spring planting season, traders and distributors obtain a better understanding of the nitrogen fertilizer mix for the year. Reports suggest a ‘normal’ nitrogen product split year (Urea, Direct application ammonia, UAN (nitrogen solutions)). The diagram below shows the average split in popular nitrogen products in key consumption states from 2010-2012. On average, 3.3 million tonnes of nitrogen are consumed through direct application ammonia. Iowa, Illinois, Nebraska and Kansas are the largest users.
Diagram 1.8: Average nitrogen fertilizer use in the United States
Nitrogen fertilizer use ('000 tonnes N)
Data: CRU, AAPFCO
With corn area forecast lower in 2015 and wheat plantings also set to decline; direct application ammonia demand is seen falling to 4.1 million product tonnes in 2015. Over the medium term, consumption is seen declining at a gradual pace, moving to 3.4 million tonnes, pressured by alternative and cheaper products such as UAN. Furthermore, aging equipment is not expected to be replaced, while expansions in plantings are expected to occur in regions where anhydrous ammonia facilities are not available.
1.5 Technical ammonia demand
1.5.1 NH3 demand from the caprolactam industry
In recent years, the caprolactam (CPL) industry has seen a sustained period of capacity growth. Much of this growth has been driven by increased demand for nylon-6 fibre which is the primary consumer of caprolactam.
The caprolactam industry is divided between facilities integrated with ammonia production and those that are not integrated, requiring merchant supply from domestic and international buyers. In countries where energy is mostly imported, such Japan or South Korea, caprolactam plants are likely to buy merchant ammonia. On the other hand, countries such as Russia and Uzbekistan mostly have integrated ammonia facilities with their caprolactam plants. Historically, the split between integrated and non-integrated facilities has been close to 50:50 and the split is expected to remain over the forecast period despite all new capacity additions expected to occur in China by 2019.
Diagram 1.9: Regional caprolactam capacity, 2014 (‘000 t)
Total production capacity stood at 6.5 million tonnes in 2014, though with the market in oversupply, actual production was lower with global operating rates in the 75 - 80% range. Capacity is expected to continue growing significantly over the forecast, though growth is likely to tail off towards the end of the market as the market becomes increasingly saturated.
The effects of increased capacity in China are also likely to affect countries actively involved in the trade of caprolactam. Increasing levels of domestic production are forecast to displace caprolactam imports into China leading to an annual import decline of 12% over the forecast period. Predictably, increased capacity in China will drive domestic demand for ammonia. By 2019, China is expected to consume 1.0 million tonnes of merchant ammonia for caprolactam production.
1.5.2 Acrylonitrile
As with the caprolactam industry, the building of acrylonitrile plants has grown to satisfy global demand for synthetic fibres and resins. Acrylonitrile is used in the production of acrylic and modacrylic fibres which account for 50% of its global use. Other uses for acrylonitrile and its polymeric derivatives include automotive components and computer appliances. Specialty plastic composites included polyacrylonitrile-based carbon fibres for lightweight applications in aircraft, motor vehicles and engineering applications. Other large consumers of acrylonitrile are the acrylonitrile (polymer), styrene and styrene acrylonitrile polymer industries. These industries account for more than 30% of the acrylonitrile used globally. They are used mainly in the production of chemical resistant industrial hoses, gaskets and seals. Global ammonia demand from the acrylonitrile industry is forecast to remain relatively constant for the majority of the forecast period. By 2019 ammonia demand is expected to reach 2.3 million t/y.
1.5.3 Methyl Methacrylate
Methyl methacrylate or MMA is expected to use approximately 158,000 tonnes of merchant ammonia globally in its manufacturing process in 2014. It is important to note that nitrogen is not consumed in the final product and the merchant ammonia is only used as an intermediary chemical to neutralize the acid conditions that the reaction favours. Ammonium sulphate is produced as a co-product from this reaction.
MMA is used mostly for the formation of acrylic plastics and a small number of specialist applications. In 2019, it is estimated that a 152,300 tonnes of ammonia will be required globally for the production of MMA, or a decrease of 3.8% since 2014 in line with growth of industrial production in developed countries.
1.6 Other Ammonia Consumers
Approximately 283,000 tonnes of ammonia are consumed globally by other minor industries in 2014. CRU has collected these processes together base on the fact that growth from these industries is not large or absolute consumption is relatively small. Included in this category is uranium processing (limited to Canada), emissions scrubbing, Nickel High Pressure Acid Leaching (Ni-PAL) and the ammonium carbonate-gypsum process. Ammonia demand in this category has a very small growth rate because many processes have been superseded by new technologies not requiring ammonia or the process has a niche that exists in a sector not showing large growth.
Ammonia demand from the above industries is forecast to increase to 308,000 t/y by 2019. Most of this new demand is to be driven by the nickel industry that is forecast to add more than 500,000 tonnes of new nickel to the current 1.6 million tonnes already produced. Almost 17% of nickel production is achieved through the Ni-PAL (requiring ammonia) process.
Chapter 2 - Supply
2.1 Outlook for Ammonia Supply
Global capacity of ammonia is expected to equal 236.4 million tonnes in 2019. This is a 0.2 million tonnes upwards revision from our previous forecast and reflects the addition of two new plants to the forecast which slightly outweighs lower than previously expected capacity growth in China. Other key supply side developments in this outlook include:
•We now have 64 projects in our committed forecast. In addition to these projects we are currently tracking 82 speculative plants with a total of 51.7 million tonnes of ammonia capacity. Out of these plants, 21 are located in East Asia, 19 in North America, 11 in South Asia, 7 in Sub- Saharan Africa with the rest spread between CIS, South America and Oceania.
•Since the previous outlook global production has been lowered to reflect a more bearish demand outlook in key ammonia consuming markets. We have lowered our forecasted ammonia demand in urea, ammonium nitrate, and ammoniated phosphate production. As a result global operating rates are now expected to reach 81.7% in 2019, a 0.6% downwards revision since the Q4 2014 version of this report.
•This chapter includes two special focus pieces looking at topical events in the ammonia market. On page 44 we look at the recent gas price increases in Pakistan and the effect on nitrogen producers there. On page 35 we have included a longer piece looking at the investment rational for the newly announced Yara and BASF joint venture ammonia plant in Newport, Texas, USA.

Supply side forecasts: The Project Gateway Methodology
In order to make its capacity forecasting methodology more transparent and clear, CRU has developed a unified system to assess announced projects in the fertilizer industry. This should not be seen as a revision to the previous methodology, but rather as an attempt to summarize and schematize it; moreover, the same methodology will be applied to all of CRU’s market outlooks with product-specific adaptations, ensuring consistency across our product range.
The Project Gateway System (PGS) is aimed at forming the basis for our capacity forecast up to a five- year horizon. Projects are divided into three groups: Speculative, Probable and Committed:
•Speculative projects are either projects at early development stages, or projects for which information is either not available or contradictory. Hence, they are not included in our base-case capacity forecast.
•Probable projects represent advanced projects that have progressed at least to the feasibility stage, but are still missing some necessary features, such as permitting or financing.
Amongst these projects, CRU identifies those which are likely to be upgraded to the committed category within the next year; the reason for this lies in our estimate for new projects to come on-stream (3-4 years). Committed capacity, summed to “advanced” Probable capacity, will form CRU’s high-case scenario.
With the aim of classifying projects according to the above categories, CRU’s PGS scores each project according to four categories:
•Raw materials and feedstock covering the sources of required production inputs.
•Finance and marketing covering the status of capital raising and agreements for the injection of production into the market, as well as feasibility studies and sustainability simulations;
•Technology and engineering covering the status of design and construction of the project;
•External factors which include the status of compulsory permits (e.g. Environmental Impact Assessments), of logistics (development of infrastructures, or access to existing ones), and social impact (stakeholder engagement, public perception of the project).
Each of these categories is assigned a numerical score, reflecting the status in each according to the matrix provided below. This exercise creates a first indication of the overall advancement of the project itself
The role of the analyst has not been reduced to the mere application of this methodology. Additional factors will play a focal role when providing the overall judgment for each project, including, amongst others:
•Whether the project has received official approval from the Board of Directors of the company;
•Type of project (greenfield vs. brownfield)
•Position of the company in the industry (junior, major or state-owned enterprise);
•Past success history of the company and historical records of comparable projects;
•Economic support of the project’s scope from CRU’s forecasts;
•Availability of publicly available information and media news about the project
Some general precautions need to be taken into account when valuing available information. For example, major companies generally release less project information into the public domain than junior companies, which are trying to raise external financing, while state-owned enterprises release even less. Less strict criteria on demonstrating availability of necessary financing is applied to major companies that are expected to fund projects primarily through free cash flow, particularly in the case of brownfield expansions.
Some general precautions need to be taken into account when valuing available information. For example, major companies generally release less project information into the public domain than junior companies, which are trying to raise external financing, while state-owned enterprises release even less. Less strict criteria on demonstrating availability of necessary financing is applied to major companies that are expected to fund projects primarily through free cash flow, particularly in the case of brownfield expansions.
The Project Gateway System (PGS): Nitrogen expansions
Whenever possible, indirect sources (such as articles in industry media) have been cross-checked and assessed, capitalizing on CRU’s experience and direct contacts in the industry. All of the categorizations are made to the best of CRU’s knowledge, and are regularly updated.
2.2 Regional Ammonia Supply Outlook
2.2.1 East Asia
2.2.1.1 China
Supply Forecast Overview
China’s ammonia production fell year-on-year in 2014. A total of 56.99 million tonnes of ammonia was produced in 2014, down by 1.8% on the 58.1 million tonnes produced in 2013. This was mostly caused by lower urea prices and lower demand in this sector. Despite this, Chinese ammonia capacity growth will remain strong over the forecast, particularly in the first two years. Nine nitrogen projects came on-stream in 2014 with a further 13 projects with (3.9 million t/y ammonia) will come on-stream in 2015. Growth is expected to tail off towards the end of the forecast and between 2017 and 2019, only 0.9 million tonnes of new ammonia capacity is expected to come on stream. In total we expect 7.8 million tonnes of new Chinese ammonia capacity over the forecast.

2.2.2 North America
Supply Forecast Overview
We are currently tracking 35 ammonia projects in North America with a total announced capacity of 14.3 million tonnes, but we are only expecting 10 of these to come on stream by 2019. This means that North American ammonia capacity will reach 42.9 million tonnes in 2019 – a 4.6 million tonnes increase on 2014. All of the new capacity in the region will be located in the USA and only three projects are being built as greenfield.
News tracker
Rentech is looking for
strategic alternatives for its ...which might include selling all its nitrogen assets.

Annual Regional Production/Capacity, million tonnes product
'000 m tonnes
Medium term outlook, million tonnes product
A new tax incentive bill was passed unanimously in the North Dakota senate in February, allowing tax exemption for the “tangible personal property”, such as building materials and building structure, used to construct or expand fertilizer production facilities in the state. This bodes well for CHS and Northern Plains Nitrogen, which are both looking to build new world scale nitrogen plants in North Dakota, but have been battling with escalating capital expenditure. The new bill is adding to an already extant bill providing tax exemptions for manufacturing equipment. Combined the two bills have the potential to provide significant savings for the two fertilizer projects should they undertake construction. North Dakota is looking to valorise its large Bakken gas endowments by incentivizing the establishment of a value-adding downstream petrochemical industry in the state. In addition to the two proposed nitrogen plants, a $4 billion Polyethylene plant has also been proposed and is in the planning stages. CHS and Northern Plains Nitrogen remain in the speculative part of our capacity tracker as both projects still need to finalise financing and EPC contracts before committing to a final investment decision.
Rentech Nitrogen Partners announced in February that it is evaluating strategic alternatives which could “include a sale of the partnership, a merger with another party, a sale of some or all of the assets of the partnership, or another strategic transaction”. Rentech has two nitrogen facilities in the USA; an ammonia, urea and UAN plant in East Dubuque, Illinois and an ammonia, sulphur and ammonium sulphate plant in Pasadena, Texas. This might be a good time to sell nitrogen assets in the USA as the cost of building is preventing new projects from materializing.
CF is the latest company to bear the burden of rising costs in the USA. The company announced in March that its brownfield expansion at Port Neal will now be $300 million more expensive than its previous estimates. The company reported that extra costs have come as the project has higher costs of labour and a need “to winterize the plant against Iowa’s cold”. The CAPEX revision does not change our forecast and we still expect the project to come on-stream in mid-2016.
While cost inflation is limiting new builds, companies continue to explore different ways of bringing extra tonnages to market without bearing the full cost of a greenfield plant. Yara and BASF recently announced that they will go ahead with a hydrogen off-gas ammonia plant in Freeport, Texas (Covered in detail below), while Agrium is considering restarting its Kenai plant in Alaska. While Agrium has obtained a key air quality permit, natural gas supply (the lack of which closed the plant in 2007) still remains an unknown in the area, and the plant is therefore not included in our forecast. When the plant closed in 2007 it had 1.3 million tonnes of ammonia capacity per year, and a restart would likely see similar volumes come stream.
Yara/BASF Freeport Ammonia Plant
High fertilizer demand and low gas prices resulting from the shale revolution have transformed the USA into the most attractive place in the world to operate a nitrogen plant. The rush to build these plants has added more than 5 million annual tonnes of ammonia since 2009. However, over the past couple of years the introduction of quantitative easing has kept rates to record lows, allowing investors access abundant cheap capital to fund new builds, leading to a boom in the US economy and a steep increase in the demand for construction services and equipment in all industries. This, in turn has had a big impact on the capital building costs for new nitrogen plants in the USA, where construction and EPC companies that have the knowledge to build these sites are making the most of this bullish market. What would have cost eight hundred million dollars a couple of years ago easily costs over a billion dollars today. As a result, the race to build ammonia plants has slowed down significantly with many projects being put on hold. We do not expect these costs to come down anytime soon and as a result, companies are looking for ways to lower their capex.
Effect of reducing capex on a Gulf Coast project's IRR
In the graph above we can see the different Internal Rates of Return that a theoretical 750,000 t/y ammonia plant in the US Gulf Coast would yield depending on the initial capex and price premium of its feedstock relative to our Long Run Henry Hub gas forecast. From the graph we can see that a plant like Dyno Nobel’s Waggaman, which cost $850 million to build and will buy its gas from Henry Hub, will yield a 12.2% annual return, a whole 2.4% higher than if it had started being built today at a cost of $1 billion. This again sheds some light on why so many projects are today being put on hold in the USA. From the graph we can also see how the IRR of a low capex project that will pay a 40% premium above Henry Hub’s price for its feedstock is higher than the IRR of a highly expensive project that will buy its gas from Henry Hub. Because of this, companies are today incentivised to look for solutions to spend less on their project capex, even if it means paying a premium for their raw materials.
The latest innovative solution comes from Yara and BASF, which announced its intention to build a 750,000 tonnes per year ammonia plant at BASF’s site in Freeport, Texas on 19 February 2015. Praxair has agreed a long-term contract to supply hydrogen, nitrogen and carbon dioxide from its Texas City complex to be used as raw materials. Without the requirement of a costly steam/methane reformer to produce hydrogen, the plant will cost significantly less than a conventional ammonia plant that uses natural gas feedstock. CRU estimates that an ammonia plant of a similar scale running on natural gas would have cost at least a billion dollars instead of the $600 million that has been budgeted.
We have run a discounted cash flow (DCF) analysis on the two options to understand how much this capital saving will allow Yara/BASF to pay for its alternative raw materials. The Weighted Average Cost of Capital (WACC) is the average return that Yara or BASF would expect to create value for their shareholders as well as to pay off any debtholders, and returns above the WACC contribute to a positive Net Present Value (NPV), indicating the relative attractiveness of each investment. Below, we show the results of this DCF analysis for a conventional ammonia plant costing $1 billion and for Yara/BASF’s preferred option as a function of the premium paid for raw materials above the Henry Hub gas price. These results are based on a WACC of 9.1% along with cost forecasts generated by CRU’s Nitrogen Cost Model and our price forecasts for ammonia and natural gas over a 25 year asset lifetime.
We estimate an NPV of just $54 million for the conventional ammonia plant but $479 million if it were to cost $600 million instead of $1 billion. In principle, Yara/BASF ought to accept to pay a premium over Henry Hub natural gas prices for its raw materials providing the NPV of the project remains above $54m. If Praxair were to charge them such a premium that the NPV drops below $54m it implies that Yara/BASF would be better off borrowing more money to build a conventional plant for $1 billion.
∆NPV ($m)

How much is too much?
Bargaining Zone
Raw materials premium
over Henry Hub
The chart above shows the difference between the NPVs of each option as a function of the premium charged by Praxair. Given our stated assumptions, we believe that Yara/BASF could pay up to 49% more for its raw materials and still create a higher NPV than by building a standalone gas-based ammonia plant. This figure is CRU’s approximation and the actual maximum premium Yara/BASF would consider paying will depend on their subjective WACC, the plant lifetime and their forecast of prices.
In practice, Yara/BASF will expect to negotiate a premium well below this maximum. Praxair’s bargaining position will be based on its average cost of production (which given that this is partly based on steam- reforming of natural gas is likely to be above the equivalent Henry Hub price) plus the additional value it believes it can extract from Yara/BASF.
Praxair is investing more than $400 million to add hydrogen and nitrogen production capacity and extend its Gulf Coast pipeline network approximately 46 miles to the Yara/BASF plant at Freeport. Notwithstanding the strategic benefits of connecting to the industrial zone in Freeport, Praxair will no doubt expect Yara/BASF to contribute to a return on this capital investments. Furthermore, Praxair will recognize that Yara/BASF will be making a large capital saving that is wholly contingent on Praxair’s supply of syngas and nitrogen and hence expect a share of this saving through the pricing of its raw material supply contract. Yara/BASF will not want to give away all the benefit of choosing this option but must accept that Praxair needs to benefit too. So although the bargaining zone for the two parties extends all the way from Praxair’s cost of production to a 49% premium over the cost of purchasing the equivalent quantities of natural gas from Henry Hub, we expect that the supply contract will be settled at a premium of 15-30%.
2.2.3 CIS
Supply Forecast Overview
Ammonia capacity in the CIS region will increase 4.4 million tonnes as seven new projects come on-stream in the next five years. Of these seven projects, five will be located in Russia, one in Azerbaijan and one in Turkmenistan. In CIS we are currently committing seven out of a total 17 tracked plants. The uncommitted plants in the region tend to be planned for the latter part of the forecast period with very little information available yet. Out of all the tracked projects, 11 are greenfield, 2 revamp and 4 brownfield.

Preparations to start construction at Shchekinoazot’s new ammonia and methanol plant in Tula, just south of Moscow, has started and the plant is included in our forecast for the first time. Haldor Topsoe is the ammonia licensor for the project, while Mitsubishi is delivering the compressors and Kirchner is providing the steam reforming furnaces. Basic design of the plant is now completed and construction work is expected to start imminently. The plant is expected to come on-stream in 2017 with 415 mtpd (~140,000 tonnes per year) of ammonia.
Navoiazot announced in March that Mitsubishi was awarded the construction tender for its planned new plant in Uzbekistan. The new complex will have capacity to produce 660,000 mt/year ammonia and 577,500 mt/year urea and the company has announced a cost of $961.74 million for the project. The project will be financed through a loan from the Fund for Reconstruction and Development of Uzbekistan of $320 million, Navoiazot's own funds and loans from local and foreign banks. The project is the latest project to be announced in Central Asia where governments have increasingly been looking to valorise stranded gas assets. While project logistics are likely to remain expensive, benefits from a low cost of natural gas (likely in the range of $1-2/MMBtu) and a favourable cost of capital (from the Fund for Reconstruction and Development of Uzbekistan), are likely to outweigh the cost. However, the project is not included in the forecast until we hear more news on the plant’s financing and technology progression.

2.2.4 Africa
Supply Forecast Overview
African ammonia capacity is expected to increase 4.6 million tonnes between 2014 and 2019. This increase will come from five new plants coming on-stream in Algeria, Egypt and Nigeria. In addition to the five plants in the forecast we are also tracking 6 further projects in the region, but it is looking increasingly unlikely that these will start production within the forecast period. African production has been revised down since the previous outlook to reflect difficult operating conditions across the region – especially in Egypt. Most of this change feeds in from a changed urea production forecast.

MOPCO has been building a brownfield 1.2 million t/y urea plant at its Damietta site since 2008. 2.2.5 South and Central America
Petrobras is currently building two ammonia plants in Brazil – Tres Lagoas which is scheduled for completion in 2016 and Uberaba the year after. Combined these plants will add 1.2 million tonnes of ammonia, though most will be used downstream in urea production. Following the large corruption scandal haunting Petrobras in recent months there has been uncertainty over the likelihood of these plants being finished on time. However, according to local news reports all disputes between the company and subcontractors are now resolved with the company guaranteeing completion of the two projects on schedule. We maintain our completion year for both plants. Once on-stream the plant will supply fertilizer and industrial demand in the region, and product from the plant will primarily be moved by truck.
Supply Forecast Overview
Three plants are forecast to come on stream in C&S; America over the forecast – two in Brazil and one in Bolivia. In Brazil, the Petrobras Tres Legoas plant is a turnkey ammonia/urea plant and will have no merchant ammonia availability. Petrobras is also building an ammonia plant in Uberaba, Minas Gerais and ammonia output from this plant is expected to feed MAP/DAP in the region, taking away some of vales ammonia demand currently imported. The bolivian plant is not expected to have any merchant ammonia, as it is already hard enough logistically to get urea from the plant to the Brazilian Cerrado, let alone ammonia.

2.2.6 Middle East
Sabic's joint venture the Ma'aden Phosphate Company (MPC) shut down its ammonia plant March 24 so that unscheduled maintenance could be carried out on its cooling facility. The ammonia unit only restarted a few days before, after a 30-day scheduled maintenance since February 22. The plant maintenance is expected to last for 10 days. In addition to this, Muntajat confirmed that Qafco's Ammonia III, V and VI plants remained shut down undergoing maintenance until the end of March leaving low ammonia inventories for April.
Supply Forecast Overview
Only one new plant is expected to come on stream in the Middle East over the next five years. This is Ma’aden’s Ras Al-Khair plant which will start commercial production in 2017. In addition to this plant we are also tracking plants in Iran, Bahrain and Saudi Arabia. Based on our preliminary export data in 2014, urea exports from Qatar are down 1 million tonnes on the previous year which is feeding through to the 2014 ammonia production number. We are currently investigating the validity of this data.

2.2.7 South Asia
The Indian government yet again dodged an opportunity make difficult decisions and tackle the country’s inefficient urea subsidy scheme in March as the new Indian budget was published with no major revisions. As a result CRU maintains our bearish view on new Indian ammonia capacity over the forecast
–despite an announcement from the Indian government that it will be looking to build more coal-fed ammonia/urea plants in coal-rich regions of the country. The only plant currently included in the forecast is the Matix plant in Panagarh which is expected to come on-stream in the end of 2015. The plant is currently experiencing problems with natural gas supply, but expects these issues to be resolved within 2- 3 months.
Supply Forecast Overview
Ammonia capacity growth in South Asia will be limited over the forecast and currently we are only forecasting two plants to start production in the region. However, all ammonia produced at these plants will be consumed in associated urea production, and as such the plants will have no effect on the traded market. There are consistently new plants being announced by the Indian government, both domestic and abroad, but so far we have not seen sufficient development in these projects to allow inclusion in the forecast.

2.3 Ammonia Production Costs and Feedstock Prices
Henry Hub gas prices have remained below $3.00/MMBtu even as extreme cold weather in parts of the north-eastern US have driven demand to record highs and pushed prices in places like New York and Boston well above $20.00/MMBtu. European gas prices have remained around $8/MMBtu during the last quarter as a warmer than average winter kept demand unseasonably low, with prices falling as low as $6.87/MMBtu in January at the Zeebrugge gas hub. However, the threat of short-term supply disruption caused gas price futures at Western European hubs to spike 2.6% on news announced during late February that Russian gas supplies to Ukraine were below half the agreed volume brokered by a European Union agreement.

2.3.1 US gas production is putting prices under pressure
The US rig count has continued to fall in response to low oil and gas prices which could have a knock on effect and limit the level of new gas supply coming on-stream over the next few months. While the natural gas rig count has only fallen 11% to 300 rigs since this time last year, oil rigs have fallen by 26% to 1,056 as low oil prices test the level of US shale operator’s profitability. Lower levels of oil production could limit associated gas production growth. Our current weighted average estimates for North American shale oil producer’s lifting costs are $65/bbl, slightly below our annual 2015 Brent forecast at $60/bbl. Furthermore, a faster than expected draw down in gas inventories over the coming months, which are currently 2.8% above the five year average at around 2,157 Bcf (mostly because of high levels of storage in underground salt caverns), due to a prolonged cold spell could provide some upside risk to our price forecast during the remainder of 2015.
Diagram 2.5: US gas production has been increasing even as monthly gas rig count falls
Data: Baker Hughes, EIA
2.3.2Weak oil market weighs on European gas prices
Waidhaus, the German border benchmark price for gas imported from Russia has been in steady decline over the last six months, currently trading around $9.30/MMBtu, the price has been following the price of Brent oil lower as the global oil markets reacts to oversupply and slowing demand. The import price of gas in Germany is negotiated on long term contracts and indexed directly to oil and/or a basket of alternative fuel prices, typically on a three to six month lag.
Waidhaus is used to determine some European industrial prices in neighbouring countries where these are set as a proportion of contract (Waidhaus) and a proportion of spot based pricing (typically the price at ZeeBrugge where prices are determined by gas-on-gas competition) with other appropriate taxes and duty on top. For example, the industrial gas prices nitrogen producers pay in Poland are closely associated
with the German border price as Poland also import gas from mostly Russia and Germany under a similar formula. Over the next six months we expect Waidhaus to continue to fall as the oil price bottoms out around $50/bbl. This is expected to yield a price at the German border of around $5.90/MMBtu during mid-2015 assuming the oil price does not see any further downwards price shocks.
The gas price at Waidhaus has a knock on effect for gas prices at European gas hubs because oil indexed gas contracts are typically priced on ‘take-or-pay’ arrangements. This requires a set volume of gas to be purchased each year under the contract. Where there is excess supply relative to demand, for instance during a milder winter than average as we have seen during 14/15, gas volumes imported above the required level of consumption are then resold into the secondary hub markets, which may depress prices if there isn’t sufficient demand to soak it up.
2.3.3 Ammonia costs
Since last quarter, we have updated our cost curves for full year 2015 cost estimates. Costs have fallen in many major regions over the last year, which has affected mostly those producers that have exposure to oil prices in their gas supply contracts, and those located in the FSU due to currency devaluation.
Nitrogen producers in Europe have seen their costs move down from being some of the most marginal producers to sit comfortably below high cost product in South East Asia and gas based producers in China. Central Europe, where the cost of gas is more closely linked to the price of oil than in Western Europe, have seen their cost base continue to fall as oil prices have fallen. As many of these gas contracts
price on a 3-6 month lag, we should start to see the $50/bbl oil price start feeding through during mid- 2015.
Western European hub markets have also fallen over the last quarter, mainly in response to higher than average levels of gas in underground storage and a warmer than expected winter which has helped loosen the supply/demand balance in the region. Furthermore, gas volumes originally purchased on take-or-pay contracts in central Europe are being sold into the secondary hub markets of Western Europe. This, along with the Euro’s recent depreciation compared to the dollar, is helping push prices below $8/MMBtu, which has given many Western European producers who buy gas on the spot market a significant cost advantage.
Diagram 2.8: Merchant Ammonia Export Cost Curve, 2015 Basis ($/t)
Data: CRU
Producers in the FSU have also seen their cost base fall, although this is mostly associated with the depreciation of the rouble compared to the dollar, where cost items such as wages, energy and rail freight are paid for in local currency making their site costs in US dollar terms fall. Domestic Russian gas prices which are regulated by the government and are denominated in roubles have fallen over the last quarter in USD terms in response to the devaluation of the Rouble which has come under pressure from international sanctions and the falling price of oil. The average industrial gas price in Russia is understood to be fixed for 2015 at 150 RUB/MMBtu but at current exchange rate expectations for 2015, around 62RUB/USD, this equates to only $2.4/MMBtu down from $3.5/MMBtu during 2014.
Producers in North America are also expected to see costs improve over the previous year. Our full year 2015, gas price estimate at Henry Hub is $3.07/MMBtu, significantly below 2014 which averaged $4.39/MMBtu.

Chapter 3 - Trade, Balance and Prices
3.1 Global Trade
Global trade is forecast to reach 19.1 million tonnes in 2019, a 5% increase on 2014. We expect lower global trade in the next couple of years as new world scale plants come online. This said, as stated in the last report, African and South-East Asian phosphate production growth in the medium run will drive an increase in ammonia imports. New capacity in Russia is also expected to increase global traded volumes.
Table 3.1: Global Exports (million tonnes ammonia)
Table 3.2: Global Imports (million tonnes ammonia)
3.1.1 North America
US ammonia imports in 2014 ended lower than the four prior years. High prices forced phosphates producer Mosaic to curbe production, and MissPhos’s bankruptcy led to low ammonia demand in the end of the year. Adding to this, severe winter weather in the Northern Cornbelt and the Dakotas halted ammonia applications much earlier than usual.
As Dyno Nobel’s plant in Waggaman comes online in 2016, we expect some of its merchant ammonia to displace Russian and Ukrainian tonnage. Ongoing gas curtailments in Trinidad are forcing Koch to import more ammonia from elsewhere. We do not expect this to change in the medium term as there does not appear to be an easy solution to the problem.
Table 3.3: North American Trade (million tonnes ammonia)
3.1.2 South Asian Imports
Indian ammonia imports ended on par with the last few years with most of it coming from Middle Eastern producers. The majority of ammonia in India is used in the Phosphate fertilizer industry for the production of DAP. Owing to high prices of phosphoric acid needed to produce DAP, producers shifted towards making NPK products in 2014, as the phosphoric acid requirements for NPKs are lower. As a
result the required ammonia was lower and imports for phosphate producers were down in 2014. In addition to the production of phosphate based fertilizer, ammonia is also used for several technical applications such as the production of nitric acid, technical ammonium nitrate, caprolactam and ammonium sulphate. The amount of ammonia used for these applications has been growing steadily over the years and last year’s increase in the production of technical ammonium nitrate compensated for the decrease in the ammonia imported by the phosphates industry.

3.1.3 East Asian Imports
East Asia mainly imports ammonia for technical uses such as the production of caprolactam and acrilonitrile. We expect the industry to grow over the years and therefore expect a slight increase in imports over the coming years.

Given the easy access to sea, South-East Asia is both an importer and exporter of ammonia. Indonesia is the largest producer of ammonia in the region and we expect them to have exported around 0.8 million tonnes and imported 0.3 million tonnes. In the medium term, we expect the region to increase their import requirements for ammonia as new phosphate production comes online in Vietnam (DAP and MAP) and Indonesia (NPKs).

we do not see these problems persisting in the long term and forecast exports from Ukraine to go back to norm by 2019.

3.2 Ammonia Price Construction
Ammonia price developments are determined by various drivers, which either have a direct impact on price volatility or contribute to global market sentiment. The influence or dominance of different factors may vary from year-to-year. Energy prices are most important for the ammonia market as they determine cash production costs. On the demand side, developments in the phosphate market are also a significant driver of ammonia prices. The major factors affecting and determining the price level of ammonia are represented in the following flowchart.
In short-term ammonia supply/demand fundamentals determine the direction of price. Understanding the supply/demand balance of gross (total) ammonia is important; however, since the bulk of global ammonia production is consumed on-site where it is produced, understanding the supply/demand balance
of merchant ammonia is more important. About 10% of the globally produced ammonia is traded internationally. Just over 25% of the merchant supply comes from Trinidad, somewhat reduced over the past two years because of curtailed gas flows to ammonia producers. Two major markets, USA and India, account for nearly 45% of global imports and their demand fluctuations play an important roles in price setting. Therefore, the interaction between demand and supply is closely monitored when forecasting prices.
3.3 Pricing Fundamentals and Price Drivers
3.3.1 Fundamentals
CRU’s nitrogen forecasting methodology is based upon a combination of underlying supply-side and demand-side fundamentals and price drivers. On the demand-side we consider crop prices as key indicator of fertilizer affordability and of future agricultural plantings. Higher crop prices will raise farm revenues and margins, triggering a supply response and increased fertilizer applications as farmers seek to improve yields and may expand harvested areas.
Table 3.10: Nitrogen Fundamentals & Price Drivers, 2012-2019
Data: CRU, Oxford Economics, IMF, USDA, APX
On the supply-side, we track the major factors of production in the regions home to the ammonia market’s marginal production; Ukraine and Eastern Europe. On the demand-side we are tracking the major broadacre grain markets (especially US corn, where direct-application ammonia is used for fertilization) as well as the DAP market (ammoniated phosphate production is one of the major downstream consumers of ammonia).
We are also following developments in the urea market where the value of the wider nitrogen market is often determined. Chinese coal prices are crucial to the price of urea in the urea market’s present state of chronic oversupply, and will have an influence on the future value of ammonia.
3.4 Ammonia Price Driver Analysis
CRU has carried out a statistical analysis of all of the supply-side and demand-side fundamentals that we track as part of nitrogen analysis and tested them for correlation with FOB Middle East ammonia prices. There are strong correlations between ammonia and fundamentals on both the supply- and demand-side; the most significant being the prices of DAP and US corn on the demand-side, and oil on the supply-side. The price of urea is also strongly correlated as the main downstream consumer of ammonia. However, as all urea is produced form captive, on-site ammonia production it is unlikely to be playing a direct role in price formation in the merchant/traded ammonia market. Rather, its role is more as a gauge of nitrogen values. Given that over 55% of the world’s nitrogen requirement is supplied as urea it is our formation that the base-value of nitrogen is set in the urea market and that other nitrogen products will value to their degree of scarcity and their favourable/unfavourable attributes (higher handling costs in the case of ammonia).
Together the price time-series for urea, DAP, corn and oil account for around 85% of the price variation in month-to-month Middle Eastern ammonia prices. We have presented standardised, long-run time series for all of these prices in the following charts (plotted against ammonia to show correlation).
Like other nitrogen products ammonia markets appear to be mean-reverting and typically supply-driven. However, as there are many downstream, non-fertilizer industries consuming urea there are periods where
ammonia prices deviate from the agricultural trends given by urea, DAP and corn markets. Having included oil, we believe that it will, to some degree, act as a proxy for other petrochemical industries’ consumption of ammonia.
CRU uses annual price forecasts of these four fundamental price-drivers to provide our analysts guidance on future price direction. As these variables only explain 85% of the ammonia price, further analysis and more qualitative analyst input is required. It should also be noted that these pricing fundamentals do not
account for marginal costs of production, nor do they account for market tightness that results from constrained capacity (tightening of the S/D balance resulting from curtailments of capacity).
Price-drivers: Ammonia Forward Guidance
•Cheap Chinese coal has caused a deflationary period in the urea market as China continues to expand its urea exports, yr-on-yr as its coal- based production costs fall.
•Most EU gas contract formulas operate on an indexation basis to the price of Brent crude with a six-month lag. In Ukraine, contracts are currently settled on a nine-month lag to Brent. And oil prices have collapsed to half of their trend value.
•Consecutive years of high output and inventory build up have put corn prices under pressure. Near ideal growing conditions in the USA, saw production move to a record 361 million tonnes in 2014, preceded by 351 million tonnes in 2013.

3.5 Global floor price calculation
Statistical analysis of the ammonia markets supply- and demand-side fundamentals can only provide us medium-term price guidance, rather than an extant price forecast. We will need to constrain prices at the lower bound with an estimate of the industry’s short-run marginal costs. That is the breakeven price of the facilities producing the last tonnes required to clear the market. Based on our current assumptions those producers would be Ukrainian.
Producers in Ukraine as well as other major producing regions in Central and East European rely on supplies of imported Russian gas as the feedstock for their ammonia production process. These gas supplies are sold on long-term take-or-pay contracts, indexed to the price of Brent crude oil on what are termed Groningen-type contracts. These contracts are based on replacement value theory: in the past oil was consumed in Europe for power-generation and heating purposes and was substitutable for gas. As
Russian gas had/has very low production costs, it was priced on an oil basis to account for its substitutability for oil in the European energy consumption mix.
In the table we have used our calculated oil-indexed gas price for Ukraine (9-month lag to our forecast of Brent crude) and modelled the weighted-average cost of ammonia exports at Yuzhnyy port. The costs are taken from CRU’s Nitrogen Fertilizer Cost Report and are on an annualised basis. This means it is an average breakeven price for the year and a producer may well, for a period, sell at a price lower than their annual breakeven price in the expectation that prices may recover in the future. There are also costs such as maintenance and payroll taxes that do not require in-year payment and may be postponed.
Table 3.11: Ukraine: Export Costs, 2014-19 ($/t)
The chart below plots our regression based ammonia price forecast at FOB Black Sea against our forecast of Ukrainian export costs. CRU’s hypothesis is that the unlike the urea market, the ammonia market is not currently chronically oversupplied and that the world will require Ukrainian ammonia production and will need to pay a price in excess of Ukrainian marginal costs in 2015 to incentivise production and export.
