3.6 Medium-term Price Forecast
Our medium-term pricing model uses the supply- and demand-side fundamentals to create a trend price over the five-year cycle. This trend price is the adjusted to factor two major considerations: market scarcity and marginal costs. As a measure of market scarcity we use our calculated global operating rate and for marginal cost we use outputs from our ammonia export cost curve generated by our Nitrogen Fertilizer Cost Model.
Table 3.12: Forecasting Ammonia Prices – Input Variables Index
•Urea Outlook: Declining raw material costs for urea via formulae based natural gas contracts and lower coal values in China have triggered a cut in CRU’s outlook for urea values over recent months. CRU is expecting weak energy prices to combine with market oversupply and remain the defining narrative in the urea market to 2017. Our lower expectations for both the Chinese coal market and the major oil benchmarks make it difficult to find support for scarcity-driven price increases much before 2018, especially as we are forecasting five new US urea plants to have begun operation by the close of 2016.
•DAP Outlook: CRU’s forecast for FOB Tampa DAP prices is tightly linked to supply-side developments. Over the medium term, two of the world’s major producers will increase capacity significantly: OCP and Ma’aden. We forecast such capacity growth to outpace growth in demand significantly, particularly for high-analysis products (DAP, MAP and TSP). At the same time, we are forecasting that Brazil to develop more domestic MAP capacity, resulting in lower imports to one of the world’s MAP largest markets. The combined action of these expansions will result in a significantly weaker international market.
•Corn Outlook: Corn prices fell sharply between April and September 2014, with the CRU corn index (2006=100) flagging from 238 to 172. Near ideal growing conditions in the largest producer – the USA – saw production move to a record 361 million tonnes, preceded by 351 million tonnes in 2013. While demand remained strong, the consecutive years ample output allowed inventories to expand to more comfortable levels, putting downward pressure on prices. Exacerbating this pressure was good production in other major growers and exporters including China, Brazil, the European Union and Ukraine. Price movements since September have been two-sided and we feel prices have found their floor. Comfortable inventory levels will mute upwards price movements over the near-term, however outlooks for lower plantings in 2015 will provide some support, as will strong demand.
•Oil: Oversupply is dominating oil markets and despite the collapse in values, the US achieved a record year for production in 2014. We are expecting annual average oil prices to halve in 2015 before returning to trend by 2018.

3.7 Risks to the forecast
Global Oil Prices Return to Trend: Our current forecast is based on an improvement in the urea supply and demand balance by 2018 and for a substantial increase in the price of oil. Our expectation in that the Brent crude benchmark price will return to trend by 2018, with prices stabilising above $100/bbl on a nominal basis. Should prices stay depressed it is likely that nitrogen values will not perform as strongly as we have forecast.
Russia/Ukraine: The situation in Ukraine looks to have stabilised this quarter and we are expecting negotiations between Russia, Ukraine and the EU to yield a stable gas price which will pass on the gains from weak oil prices to Ukrainian producers. However, should the situation worsen and Russia starve Ukraine of energy or enforce a price for gas in the $11-13/MMBtu range prices at Black Sea may increase in-line with increasing marginal ammonia costs.
Iranian Sanctions: Western sanctions are having a real effect on the ability of international urea consumers to import product from the country. However, trade has far from ceased. CRU considers that in the short-term the lack of Iranian supply is largely factored into price expectations and therefore will have little positive effect. The scale of new capacity being commissioned elsewhere will also offset upside potential. The main pricing risk in the medium-term would be on the downside of pricing. Should trade restrictions on Iran be eased or removed in the medium-term the price effect would likely be negative. The need to regain market share at a point when large scale Iranian trade could return would result in downwards price pressure.
North African Output: However, North Africa continues to be an unreliable supplier and should imported LNG supplies not reach export-orientated, Egyptian ammonia producers availability in the Mediterranean and Atlantic may suffer. The Algerian government has also been obstructive with the issuance of export licenses.
Appendix A - The Long Term Outlook for the Ammonia Market
The primary purpose of CRU’s Ammonia Market Outlook is to provide a medium-term view of pricing dynamics in the global ammonia market. We define the medium-term as a period within which there is a reasonable ceiling for ammonia supply potential, set by our understanding of existing ammonia capacity and our view of committed future capacity. That is to say, all currently operational ammonia facilities, as well as the committed new investment projects that we believe will begin operation within a foreseeable timeframe. The length of our medium-term outlook is therefore limited to the period of time required to scope and assess the feasibility of a project and the time required for construction and commissioning; we estimate this timeframe to be five years.
In the short term, supply and demand decisions are independent of one another. Producers’ only available response to changes in demand is to control their variable costs, which are dependent on output volumes. At least one input cost is fixed, though in reality it is more likely that on a short-term basis several inputs are effectively quasi-fixed (e.g., labour, interest on debt and central overheads) and independent of the level of output. This means that operating decisions are based on the relationship between market prices and operating costs; in other words producers will make what they can of available raw materials, energy and production capacity. At the same time, demand decisions are a function of global economic activity. Accordingly, the main risk to our medium-term outlook is that significant imbalances between supply and demand inevitably lead to a sharp fluctuation in price. We call this “cyclical market risk”.
However, in the longer term, markets are self correcting. Periods of high prices encourage producers to invest in additional ammonia capacity. Whereas periods of low prices lead to a lack of investment in the market’s supply-side (and may encourage additional consumption). Therefore, over the long term, ammonia prices trend towards an average level that is set by the industry’s fundamental supply characteristics. Resource availability (gas and coal availability) and technological advancement are the major risks to the longer-term outlook and we refer to this as “structural risk”.
Our main assumptions when assessing long-term ammonia pricing dynamics are threefold:
•Firstly, that food consumption and economic growth will determine demand for nitrogen fertilizers, while industrial productivity and technological development will provide the basis for non-agricultural nitrogen demand
•Secondly, that there is an implied supply gap based on our view of foreseeable capacity (existing supply and committed future supply) and the forecast of long-term ammonia demand
•And finally, that supply will respond to this implied market scarcity and resulting ammonia price increases with new capacity investments, the operating and capital costs of which will provide the basis for the ammonia price trend over the long run
A.1 The long-term outlook for ammonia demand
We have taken our global nitrogen fertilizer demand forecast to 2018 as the starting point for our long- term forecast and made long-term projections based on required growth in agricultural production and industrial production forecasts. An explanation of the work that underpins our forecast of agricultural production is provided in the following pages. In using the projections for agricultural production as a basis for our ammonia fertilizer forecast, we have made two key assumptions:
1.We have assumed that fertilizer consumption moves in line with agricultural production.
Since the end of the Green Revolution in the late 1970s, there has been extremely high correlation between consumption of mineral fertilizers and agricultural production. This is particularly clear if fertilizer consumption in the USSR is removed from the data – central planning policies in the USSR saw fertilizer consumption far beyond the requirements of agriculture from the mid-1960s to the dissolution of the Soviet Union in 1992. The diagram below shows that the global five-year average intensity of fertilizer use has been 21.5-24.0 kg nutrient per tonne of agricultural production since 1980, a period in which the world’s agricultural production has doubled.
It is of course possible that some structural change will cause this relationship to break down, but this would require some major technological innovation far beyond the incremental improvements in non- fertilizer drivers of agricultural production that we are seeing today. The possibility that the impact of increases in fertilizer use on yields diminishes (particularly in developing economies) only serves to put greater pressure on expanding cultivated land area in order to meet the forecast requirements of agricultural production.
Diagram A.1: World Fertilizer Nutrient Consumption (1968-2013)
Note: excludes fertilizer consumption in the USSR up to its dissolution in 1992.
2.Having determined a forecast of total nutrient consumption, we estimate how nutrient ratios may change given projected changes to crop mix and to nutrient intensity.
Nitrogen consumption growth outstripped consumption of other nutrients for many years up until the early 1990s. Since then, nutrient ratios have stabilized, and indeed potash showed signs of reversing this trend in the early 2000s. However, the sharp increase in potash and phosphate prices since 2007 has impacted demand for these nutrients and consumption growth has again fallen behind nitrogen. But in the long-term, we regard today’s nutrient ratio as conservative and believe that there is scope for potash consumption growth to outperform nitrogen consumption growth as a result of changes in the global crop mix and a move in developing countries to more balanced fertilizer use, reducing reliance on nitrogen.
Table A.1: N Consumption Forecast Relative to P&K;
Note that in the last edition of this Report, the fixed N:P:K ratio case was presented as the base case, with a range of downside scenarios. We are now presenting the fixed N:P:K ratio case as a high case, and the base case now includes some balancing of the global N:P:K ratio. As a result of this, the long-term nitrogen fertilizer consumption and ammonia demand forecasts presented subsequently are substantially lower than those in the last Report.
The ratio of potash to phosphate consumption has been much more stable historically, and while some fluctuation is inevitable, we do not expect any structural shift in the global P2O5:K2O ratio over the forecast period.
Diagram A.2: Nitrogen fertilizer and urea consumption forecast
Diagram A.2 above shows CRU’s long-term forecast for total nitrogen consumption, as well as for ammonia; both shown in million tonnes of nutrient. As can be seen, ammonia has become increasingly more important in supplying the nitrogen requirement for crop application, both as a result of its increased availability globally and thanks to new developments providing more constant nutrient release subsequent to its application.
We forecast this trend to increase over time, reaching 63% of total nitrogen demand by 2038, based on the expectation that a more competitive market and higher global availability will play in favour of ammonia on a world scale. Regional developments might very well differ from this, as is the case for recent demand growth for UAN1 and ammonium sulphate in the USA. CRU has the view that the effects of localised changes in fertilizer preference are not expected to significantly alter the forecast for global nitrogen balances.
Non-fertilizer uses for ammonia include resins and adhesives, animal feed, flame retardants, de-icing and stabilization of explosives, which are assumed to grow following our macroeconomic projections for global industrial production. A major source of growth is forecast to come from the use of ammonia in the abatement of toxic nitrogen oxides (NOx) from vehicle exhaust and various stationary emitters.
Our total solid ammonia forecast is shown in the Diagram A.3 below, which also depicts the breakdown of ammonia into technical and fertilizer use.
Diagram A.3: Gross ammonia demand forecast to 2038
1Our forecast reflects solid urea for direct application and use in NPK compounds only, hence the solution used in UAN production is not part of the urea forecast.
Focus on long term outlook for food and agricultural markets
Demand for fertilizers is determined primarily by the outlook for production of agricultural crops, which is closely related to food consumption, as well as the need for animal feed, natural fibres and biofuels. Taking into account these factors, in order to forecast medium term fertilizer consumption, CRU uses a bottom-up forecast of nutrient demand, based on forecasts of food demand and harvested areas. These forecasts consider factors such as crop plantings, applications rates (which are affected by crop prices and fertilizer prices), economic cycles and population growth (and in turn changes in income and diet).
Over the long term it becomes more difficult to predict the development of many of these variables, especially the timing or duration of economic cycles driving income changes. Instead, the focus of analysis is on the structural elements that determine trend levels and rates of growth through time. The drivers of fertilizer demand that will persist over the long term will be food consumption (driven by population growth and changes in income) and changes in productivity. There are also issues that deserve consideration in order to appreciate whether there could be any significant changes in these structural drivers, such as resource availability, technological advances, and climate change.
CRU appreciates that these are significant issues with wide ranging implications that cannot be fully investigated within the context of this report. However, it is important that we understand the main issues facing participants of agricultural markets and the following section provides an overview for agricultural markets and fertilizer demand over the long term (2019-2039).
Food consumption
Over the long run, population growth and income growth will determine food consumption. CRU has examined food demand measured by calorific intake as a function of per capita GDP. A 2007 study from the Food and Agricultural Organization of the United Nations (FAO) shows that as income per capita rises in developing countries, their calorific intake also increases rapidly, but over time growth in calorific intake does not keep pace with growth in income.
Changes in income also impact people’s diets. As income rises, people tend to spend money on more expensive, better tasting foods – moving away from staple foods, such as cereals, to consuming to more meat, edible oils, and dairy products. For example, according to the FAO, from 1990-2007 incomes in East Asia rose by 6.0% p.a.: during this time consumption of meat and fish rose by 3.6% p.a. and consumption of eggs and dairy products rose by 5.9% p.a., while consumption of cereals and root vegetables actually fell. The switch towards more meat consumption requires higher levels of agricultural production and resource use, so total grain production per capita rises as income increases.
CRU has made forecasts of future food consumption, as measured by nutritional intake (kilocalories), based on the position of key regions on the curve, and projections for growth in income per capita (driven by population and GDP forecasts). This means that those regions with lower incomes per capita are assumed to experience faster growth rates in food consumption than those with higher incomes per capita.
The forecast for food consumption assumes that the world population will grow from 7.1bn to 8.9bn by 2039, equivalent to 0.9% p.a. This growth is expected to be driven by lower income countries, with Africa contributing 820 million to total growth. CRU’s forecasts for GDP are a function of working populations and labour productivity. Growth will increasingly be driven by the developing economies, which can still support relatively rapid labour productivity growth, and hence GDP growth.
Based on population and income growth projections, CRU forecasts world food consumption (measured by calorific intake) to reach 9,900 trillion kcal by 2039, compared to 7,300 trillion kcal in 2013.
Data: FAO, CRU
Agricultural production
Whilst crop production is subject to seasonal and cyclical variations, such as the influence of unpredictable weather conditions and changes in crop prices, over the long-term, production growth in agricultural crops tracks demand. However, will agricultural producers be able to feed 8.9 billion people the 9,900 trillion kilocalories CRU forecasts will be needed by 2039? CRU believes that the ability of agriculture to meet future demand for food will be principally governed by resource constraints and agricultural productivity and these issues are considered below.
(i) Resource constraints
CRU believes that the most notable resource constraints to future agricultural production are the availability of land and water. Agricultural producers need to increase agricultural land area and/or increase yields, but there are concerns that the availability of arable land may become a limiting factor.
The FAO’s Global Agro-Ecological Zones (GAEZ) study estimates that around one third of the world’s land surface is of prime land (capable of yields of more than 80% of the “potentially attainable” maximum) and good land (capable of 40-80% of potentially attainable yields) for rain-fed agriculture. This potential land for rain-fed crop production totals around 4.5 billion ha, of which 3.2 billion ha is still not yet under cultivation. Whilst this seems to provide ample scope for agricultural expansion, in reality much of this land is not presently suitable for crop production due to the following reasons:
1)the land is already under an alternative competing use, such as woodland, urban areas, infrastructure;
2)the available land is unevenly distributed, with the majority in sub-Saharan Africa and Latin America;
3)the land available may not be suitable for the crops that are most in demand;
4)the infrastructure necessary to support large-scale farming may inadequate or absent altogether.
CRU believes in addition to any physical constraint on land area, there is also an economic constraint (i.e. there could be land available but it might be uneconomic to farm, subject investment costs, land quality and prevailing crop prices) that will be reached earlier. As this economic limit is approached it will become increasingly slow and costly to bring new marginal land into production – meanwhile crop yields will need to rise significantly to compensate for the constraint on available arable land. Based on current literature, CRU is of the opinion that overall the world will not reach the physical and economic bottleneck in land availability within the forecast period (to 2037), but shortages of arable land will pose problems in some areas, notably North Africa and South Asia. Moreover, there will need to be the incentives in place, including higher crop prices and public investment, in order to enable the substantial investment needed to create roads, silos and other requirements, as well as for farm machinery, agronomy, herbicides, pesticides and significant nutrient applications to make available land productive.
Water scarcity is a complex issue, on account of the many competing demand sectors, such as residential, agricultural and industrial. The agricultural sector is by far the biggest consumer of water, but its share of global freshwater withdrawals has trended downwards since the late 1980s. Nonetheless, according to the FAO, there are still ample opportunities to increase water use efficiency, through better water resource management, provided the necessary incentives are in place.
On balance, given the high productivity of irrigated land, CRU believes that constraints on water resources represent a significant risk to demand growth for food and fertilizers over the forecast period. Despite there being sufficient water resources at a global level, the FAO and other bodies do stress the inequality in distribution of water resources, and the fact that an increasing number of countries are facing dangerous levels of water scarcity, most notably in North Africa, South Asia and parts of China. Moreover, greater climate variability owing to climate change provides a greater degree of uncertainty, meaning regions could experience more periods of severe water shortages or flooding.
(ii) Agricultural Productivity
Facing resource constraints, the main means by which agricultural producers can meet future demand is by increasing productivity. According to the FAO, over 1961 to 2009, more than three quarters of the world’s growth in crop production can be attributed to improvements in agricultural yields, as opposed to land expansion and increased cropping intensity. However, what is of most concern is: can further
improvements be made in agricultural productivity in order to enable continued increases in average yields over the long term?
There has historically been a clear correlation between GDP per capita and crop yields, implying that further, possibly greater, improvements can be expected based on our forecasts for income growth. Many countries still have huge scope to bridge the gap between attainable and actual yields through improvements in the use of technology, crop husbandry, fertilization and irrigation, and agronomic best practice (including nutrient application). Although there are concerns that further gains in productivity might not be possible from a technical perspective or that resource constraints may counter productivity gains, current literature suggests that further yield improvements are possible. However, the pace of change will certainly be dependent on public policy and investment measures to incentivise the necessary development. Although some efforts to improve productivity may in fact have negative implications for fertilizer consumption, such as precise application techniques to minimize losses, fertilizer use will continue to play an important role in productivity gains in many countries.
Aggregate yield (t/ha)
CRU has made a high level forecast of future agricultural production based on its food consumption forecast and assumptions about the ability of agricultural output to grow and meet future demand. By taking into account changes in income, it allows for dietary changes over the long term, but it does not allow for any unforeseen step changes in output growth rates, owing to wealth effects. CRU forecasts
agricultural production to grow by 1.4% per annum over 2019 to 2039, rising from 8.4 billion tonnes in 2014 to 12.0 billion tonnes in 2039.
It should be stressed that CRU’s forecasts allow for some agricultural production being driven by non- food uses, most notably biofuel production. World biofuel output experienced exceptional growth of 22% per annum on average over 2000 to 2011, but this rate is now slowing as governments exercise more caution in their policy support for biofuels compared to in the early 2000s. It is worth remembering that biofuel crops often yield other products (such as soyabean meal or distiller's dried grains), which are used in the food chain.
Risks to the Outlook
The forecast presented above represents CRU’s Base Case Scenario for long term consumption and production of food, which is used to drive our long term forecasts for fertilizer demand. However, CRU recognises forecasting consumption over such a long period of time is fraught with risks and uncertainties, which could result in significantly different scenarios.
•Stronger than expected energy prices or wider policy support for wider biofuel initiatives could increase the share of industrial demand in future agricultural production and accelerate the period in which we reach the economic and physical constraint in available arable land.
•CRU appreciates that the long term impact of climate change is hugely uncertain and has assumed that the net macroeconomic effect of climate changes and measures to tackle it are neutral. However, we expect a greater unpredictability and extremity in weather events, and hence crop production, over the long term.
•There are upside and downside risks associated with biotechnology and the introduction of new farming methods and technologies. An increase in yields resulting from such technologies serves to boost absolute crop production, and allows some marginal land to come back into use – both factors are positive for long term fertilizer consumption. Conversely, many technologies aim at using bespoke formulations and targeted application to maximize the effect of fertilizers and minimize physical wastage, resulting in lower fertilizer unit consumption.
•The exact impact of past and future agriculture and industry on the world’s biodiversity cannot yet be fully appreciated. There is the possibility that greater than expected environmental degradation of resources, such as water and land, could result in constraints on agricultural production being brought forward, and could limit the gains possible in productivity. An example of a danger to biodiversity is the noted fall in bee numbers, which could result in lower levels of pollination and in turn reduced fertility over the long term, if sustained.
A.2 Long-term guidance: CRU’s LRMC methodology
To provide longer-term ammonia price guidance beyond the five-year horizon of our cyclical forecast, CRU has provided a forecast of the industry’s long-run marginal costs (LRMC). Our LRMC methodology is based primarily on the assumption laid out in the previous section; that in the long-run prices will always trend towards the LRMC in a competitive market. Furthermore, we assume that the LRMC represents the economic cost (including capital charges) of building the market clearing (last tonnes required) ammonia capacity required to meet demand in each given year. However, it should be noted that our forecast of LRMC is not intended to provide a literal forecast of ammonia prices in each given year. Rather our intention is to provide a long-term trend or equilibrium price, about which the cyclically determined ammonia price will fluctuate over time.
During commodity price cycles we fully expect the price to increase above or below the LRMC. There could be any number of drivers of this disconnect including structural raw material shortages (gas availability), unforeseen additional global consumption and unexpected production losses (civil unrest, war, natural disasters). When the price is above this level we expect additional investment to be attracted into the sector, leading to more ammonia capacity, lower nitrogen pricing and lower returns. This helps to drive the price back towards its long run attractor. Conversely, there will be periods when we expect the price to fall below the LRMC. Plant construction may be undertaken under expectations of growth and price scenarios that do not materialise. Overcapacity may become an issue driving price setting away from the LRMC. Under these circumstances capital is not attracted to the industry, new plants are not built (or delayed) and closures may be accelerated. Ultimately this should lead to a tighter market, a higher nitrogen pricing and a reversion towards the LRMC trend price.
To calculate LRMC we must consider three main elements:
•Operating costs of new world-scale ammonia plants built in representative target countries. These countries have been selected on the basis that investors in the nitrogen industry will be attracted to regions that have access to energy supplies (fuel and feedstock) and capital at competitive rates.
•The capital expense of building new world-scale ammonia plants and the weighted-average cost of capital used to determine the capital charge per tonne. As outlined in the previous bullet point, this is often a regional consideration. The capital charge per tonne will be determined by regional engineering and construction costs and a cost of capital that will reflect the minimum return that equity and debt holders will require for bearing risk.
•The future rate of change in these variables. LRMC is not a single fixed price and will evolve as input costs and capital costs inflate (or deflate) over our forecast period. We have developed a weighted basket of inflation factors that will determine the rate of increase in our nominal LRMC forecast.
A.3 Choice of regions for LRMC
CRU takes the average economic costs of ammonia production in five regions to represent global LRMC in our base year (2012). The economic costs are determined by representative greenfield/brownfield standalone ammonia plants, the details of which can be found in the following table. We have selected the FSU, the Middle East, North Africa, Sub-Saharan Africa and the USA as regions where we foresee plants being built over the next 25 years.
The basis for selecting these regions is their ready access to supplies of competitively priced hydrocarbon feedstock (natural gas and coal) and access to capital at a reasonable rate. We have excluded China and India from our LRMC for structural reasons, despite both countries currently (China) or recently (India) attracting investment. Years of tariff gas pricing in India have eroded the business case for private-sector investment in domestic gas production and distribution infrastructure. The country is now chronically short of gas and has resorted to the international LNG market for its balance of supply. While we do not think investment in Chinese plants is a purely economic consideration given the government’s clear desire to promote the expansion of capacity to create a domestic oversupply situation. Longer-term, China will need to address its worsening air quality problems, the result of its huge coal consumption.
Central Asia aside, Sub-Saharan Africa and the MENA remain the last regions where gas can still, to some extent, be considered a stranded resource. Gas production is often associated (with oil production) and Iran and Qatar possess the largest reserves of natural gas after Russia. While Iran’s recent geopolitical isolation limits its investment opportunities in the near-term, the Persian Gulf and Algeria remain attractive to investors, chiefly on the back of the state’s active role in limiting gas price tariffs to below $1.50/GJ to encourage domestic consumption. Our outlook for MENA gas prices is a real-terms increase over the LRMC forecast period to account for a shift to non-associated gas production as associated fields wind-down and require greater re-injection of gas to recover marginal barrels of oil. Sub-Saharan Africa clearly has plentiful hydrocarbon resources as well as convenient freight access to export markets in India and Brazil, although the level of political risk, lack of infrastructure and the problem of corruption do lead to higher country risk premiums and capital charges. However, on balance, we think that improving institutions of state, infrastructure investment and the development of civil society are supporting growing foreign direct investment (FDI) and that capital inflows to develop initially export-orientated nitrogen capacity is very likely. Given structural tightness in the merchant ammonia market, some level of dedicated merchant supply would also appear likely.
It is our view that Russia does not require significant additional capacity to support domestic ammonia consumption. However, the country’s ammonia production base is in need of modernisation. It is this recycling of existing capacity for efficiency gains, coupled with incremental domestic demand that will necessitate the new brownfield ammonia projects over the LRMC forecast period (the project in our baseyear calculation represents a brownfield expansion). The scale of US ammonia import dependence currently stands at around 6.0 million tonnes.
A.4 LRMC base year: 2013$
The LRMC calculation is based on two main cost components: business costs and economic costs. Business costs are calculated on a cash costs basis, before being adjusted to include additional costs associated with the sales and marketing of the ammonia, central overheads and working capital. Full economic costs include an additional capital charge to account for the market value of the asset over its remaining productive lifetime, charged at the weighted average cost of capital (WACC).
Table A.2: Ammonia Business and Economic Costs (real 2013$)
*includes annual sustaining capital, charged at 1.5-3.0% of the assets’ market value
Our base year is constructed using a “typical plant” approach, whereby we have based our plant assumptions on a world-scale 2,050tpd basis for the non-US plants and a 2,200tpd configuration for the US plant. All costs are based on CRU’s nitrogen cost methodology. Our assumption is that all of the NGSR plants have a gas conversion efficiency of 31MMBtu/tNH3, equivalent to 32.7GJ/tNH3. Steam, power, labour and consumable charges are fully broken out and accounted for in the previous table. CRU’s business costs methodology requires that maintenance, sustaining capital and central sales and admin expenses be charged on a per tonne basis.
Our business costs are calculated with an additional realisation charge or credit. This net realisation adjusts the business costs at all four plants on a Yuzhnyy basis for direct comparison and allows us to calculate the global LRMC. Essentially all production costs have been netted-back to Yuzhnyy, allowing for differences in land and ocean freight to each plant’s target market.
Our economic costs are based on the plant’s market value, amortisation period and the industry weighted average cost of capital (WACC). We have estimated each plant’s market value based on observed, real- world announced capital expenditure (capex) and have then made a 10% upwards adjustment to account for typical construction overruns. A portion of the plant’s capital expense needs to be charged during the construction period and this is again accounted for in the final capex numbers in the plant specifications above. The capex is amortised over 25 years to account for the plant’s useful lifetime (we assume that there is no terminal value, i.e. scrap value is at most equivalent to the cost of a shutdown) and charged at the real WACC. One final step when calculating the full economic costs for the LRMC is to tax the return to equity at the local corporate tax rate (dividends returned to equity holders are taxable). To avoid double counting the tax shield on financing (inherent in the WACC), the implied cash flows must be treated as if each project were 100% equity financed.
Our WACC is calculated using 20-year US Treasury bills as our benchmark for the risk free interest rate and the total return on the S&P; 500 index over 40 years for the return to equity in the cost of equity component. We have calculated our beta coefficient on a US basis, using an average beta of listed US companies, while the cost of debt is based on corporate debt yields of the major industry players. This method provides our baseline industry WACC. A country risk premium ranging from 1% to 3% has been added to the WACC to account for regional risk as perceived by an international investor (local investors may well use a lower WACC as perceptions of risk vary).
Over 75% of the cost components in the base year are accounted for by energy costs and capital charges. This means that the rate of LRMC escalation over the 25-year forecast will be determined by energy price and capital cost inflation. It also means that investors will be seeking to control costs by building in areas with cheap construction and labour costs as well as access to secure, low priced gas (or coal).
A.5 LRMC inflation indexes
Our global average LRMC base year is forecast to 2038 using an inflation index derived from a weighted basket of cost inputs (table and chart below). We have divided the economic cost profiles outlined in the previous section into five indices, weighted to represent their proportion of overall economic cost. These are capital, energy, labour, supplies and ocean freight, forecasts, generated by CRU’s in-house economics team. It should be noted that our LRMC inflation index does not directly account for foreign exchange movements against the dollar. However, all of our original forecasts for energy and labour are denominated in local currency before being adjusted to nominal dollar terms. We have made an allowance for technological advancement and efficiency gains; the LRMC inflation index is discounted for a gradual shift from 31.0 to 29.0 MMBtu/tNH3 and ammonia plant capacity creep from 2,500tpd to 3,500tpd over the forecast period.

Data: CRU
Capital inflation: The index is calculated using CRU’s expectations for chemical plant specific capital expense and includes construction, equipment and local inputs. Currently we expect an annualised growth rate of 1.8% over 25 years (below our US GDP deflator indicating a long-term real decrease in costs). Chemical plant equipment costs follow similar trends to other process equipment costs such as mining and metallurgy; these are underpinned by their exposure to macroeconomic fundamentals. In the medium term, chemical equipment costs are forecast historically high levels, though still remaining below the current level of inflation. While currently elevated above historical levels, our expectation is that equipment costs will remain relatively flat as supply constraints weaken. Construction and labour costs are likely to rise over the medium term, especially as our forecast for emerging market currencies appreciates against the dollar. However, labour and construction costs are far lower for the chemical industry than that of mining and other heavy industries and as such this is expected to have a limited affect on our modelled index.
Energy and Resources: Over the long term, CRU expects to see gas become increasingly commoditised by greatly expanded LNG supply. Gas supply increases in North America from unconventionals will help fuel this expansion, as technological advances continue decreasing the cost of producing the country’s shale and coal bed methane resources. A more competitive and liquid LNG market is expected to reduce the real prices in West and Central Europe over the long term. The Asia Pacific regions reliance on LNG imports is expected to remain and the region will continue to be the price-setting region for LNG contracts. We have aggregated gas prices in these regions and weighted them by expected consumption. We expect gas prices across these regions to grow at an annualised rate of 4.3% which is expected to be the second fastest growing inflator to our LRMC calculation behind that of labour costs.
Middle Eastern and North African countries, which currently have plentiful supplies of cheap natural gas, and are expected to continue monetising this resource through LNG and nitrogen production. The movement away from associated gas production towards dedicated production should raise regional gas extraction costs. Prices are expected to undertake real-terms increases in MENA reflecting higher production costs. However, we still expect gas prices to fertilizer producers to remain the most competitive in the world.
In Russia, increased domestic competition has the potential to lower the cost of gas exports to Central and Western Europe and with slow demand growth in these regions, Russian gas producers are expected to seek higher revenues in their domestic market. As such, we expect the domestic Russian gas price to increase over the medium term, towards export parity pricing, implying a significant downside risks to fertilizer producers’ margins.
Chemical Supplies: CRU’s chemical supplies index represents inflation from both services and site consumables (such as parts and non-process materials) required to keep the business functioning and the plant operating at its optimum efficiency. The weighted average index of countries is expected to inflate at 1.9%, just below the level of GDP inflation.
Ocean Freight: Ocean freight rates over the long term will be linked to the price of bunker fuel and time- charter rates for large ocean going vessels. Bunker fuel prices are expected to remain relatively flat over the medium-term forecast period. Based on CRU’s medium-term estimates for the EU’s ETS carbon trading scheme, we see little upside risk to the price of bunker fuel. However, over the longer term, we expect bunker fuel prices to rise significantly as the ETS legislation is tightened and carbon prices rise. Time charter rates are expected to increase over the long term. However, in the short term, prices are set to remain below historical values as pressure from global oversupply weighs on pricing.
A.6 Long-run marginal cost forecast, 2013-2039
The results of our global ammonia LRMC forecast can be seen in the charts below, based on our current view of the ammonia market, likely regions for capacity expansion and our forecasts of energy price inflation. As such, this forecast is very dependent on the regions selected to attract additional ammonia projects. Should these regions change, for example if access to competitively priced gas is exhausted, we could have a higher average cost structure in our base year and a higher LRMC.
Rather than average all of the regions’ economic costs to produce our base-year urea LRMC, we have taken an average of the marginal, market clearing country/regions weighted by production. In this instance, our LRMC is based on the full economic costs of greenfield plants in Russia and the USA. This also means that our inflation index only includes gas, labour, power and consumables price forecasts for these regions. So, our base-year 2012 LRMC of $342/t at Yuzhnyy and is inflated to an LRMC of $6940/t in 2038.
At the time of writing, the wider nitrogen market is moving towards oversupply and ammonia prices are moving closer to the market’s short-run marginal costs (SRMC) on an fob Black Sea basis. The LRMC has typically been lower than SRMC as efficiency gains and access to cheap gas supplies at new greenfield plants are offset by significant capital servicing charges incurred by ammonia plants that are moving closer to $1 billion on a capex basis, while older, less efficient existing plants at the margin continue to operate on a mostly debt-free basis. We currently consider producers in Europe and Ukraine to be the market clearing tonnes, setting a support price of around $390-420/t. Ease of access to large domestic agricultural and chemical markets, and political concerns about job and food security have kept 1970’s and 1980’s vintage plants open when they perhaps would otherwise have closed.
Having the markets SRMC above LRMC over the last five years has encouraged record levels of urea investment in China, the USA, MENA, the FSU and South East Asia. And a full project pipeline has the potential to add upwards of 40.0 million tonnes of efficient, low cost new solid urea capacity well. However, the merchant ammonia has not seen nearly as much investment and the market remains relatively tight (relative to urea) and we are not expecting to see closures of merchant ammonia capacity unless it is heavily integrated with a loss-making urea plant. However, given the debt-free status of these marginal urea/ammonia plants and the low, if any, residual capital value of a marginal plant (with the potential to return negative cash flows), we would expect a slow rate of displacement. This rate of displacement is further slowed by non-economic considerations. Many plants in China, the Balkans and the FSU play an important societal role supporting employment in their locality, as well as propping up precious foreign currency reserves. Also, the survival of these marginal tonnes will to some extent be supported by the real-terms increase in ammonia values our forecast is suggesting.
One last thought, with prices falling to SRMC and the medium-term outlook weakened by the prospect of oversupply, investment should slow (indeed, weaker investment fundamentals are already causing delays or cancellations to projects in scoping or feasibility). But that doesn’t mean that we would expect investment to grind to a halt, or for marginal producers’ lifetime to be extended, as there is still a case for investors in particularly low-cost regions to capture profits found below the pricing level set by the industry’s SRMC. A producer in the MENA region for example, could build and operate a profitable plant with the ammonia market pricing in the $350-400/t range, earning a profit in excess of its operating expenses, business costs and returns to debt and equity holders at the project WACC.
Table A.4: LRMC forecast, 2013-2039 ($/t)
Appendix B - Industrial and economic outlook, 2015-2019
•Weak growth and soft inflation prompts rash of rate cuts
•Policy divergence means €/$ parity and yen at ¥130 by Q4
•Poor data bring China’s downside risks back into spotlight
B.1 Overview
The US Federal Reserve removed the word “patient” from its statement on 18th March – a reference to the central bank’s approach to tightening monetary policy. The move effectively set the US on course for higher interest rates later this year. The Fed also downgraded its interest rate forecasts, leading us to expect that rates will begin to be hiked from September, not June. Central banks around the world, including those in China, India, Thailand, Indonesia, South Korea and Australia have loosened monetary policy. While the moves are partly reactions to expected US rate hikes, looser policy is also motivated by combinations of weaker than expected economic growth, soft inflationary pressures, and weaker than expected demand from China.
Monetary policy divergence is a central theme of our forecasts. Aside from in Asia, other major central banks are also in easing mode. The ECB’s Quantitative Easing (QE) programme began on 9th March and looser policy has already weighed on the single currency. We’ve significantly lowered our euro forecast this month and we now expect a euro to be worth $1.00 by the end of this year, from €/$0.92 ($/€1.08) forecast last month.
As a share of GDP no other central bank is expanding its balance sheet on the scale of the Bank of Japan. With CPI likely to turn negative again soon, and with the BoJ determined to raise inflation expectations, we now expect a second extension to QE in Q2. Anticipating this decision we’ve weakened the yen by 10% throughout the forecast and expect the yen will fall to ¥130 against the US$ by the end of this year, from our previous forecast of ¥119.
China has started the year poorly with industrial production growth especially weak. Over January and February, IP growth fell to 6.8% y/y, from 7.6% in Q4 with the main drag on growth coming from the real estate downturn. In our view, if the government’s target for GDP growth of “around 7%” is to be met, policy will need to become more supportive. In our feature this month, we’ve reviewed the March meeting of the 12th National People’s Congress (NPC), an annual event at which key objectives are set and main risks identified for the year ahead.
Our forecast of global GDP growth is little changed from last month. Our expectations of softer industrial production growth in China and also in the US this year has pushed down our global IP growth
forecast to 3.2% from 3.6% last month. In China we’re now forecasting IP growth of 7.5% this year (from 7.9% last month) and in the US we expect 3.5% IP growth (from 4.3%). We view China’s recent run of poor data as a growing downside risk to our global forecast and have analysed an investment crunch in our scenario.
Upside risks
•US recovery takes-off: The US economy could finally take-off, bringing a stronger economic rebound as pent up domestic demand is released. Stronger world trade would benefit all, especially export- dependent emerging economies.
•Indian economic renaissance: The government used its strong mandate to launch a positive Union Budget for this financial year. If reforms are implemented more quickly than expected, India could return to 8%+ GDP growth.
•Oil prices permanently lower: Crude oil is 50% lower than seven months ago, providing a net boost to the global economy. Staying at these levels for longer, cheap oil would boost global GDP growth by 20-30bp pa over 2015-17.
Downside risks
•China crash: A massive increase in debt and a bold program of structural reforms mean that risks are increasingly skewed to the downside as we go forward, especially for investment and construction A large negative risk for commodity markets.
•Eurozone stagnation: The region is recovering but Greece’s financial problems are unresolved and could trigger a renewed recession made harder to escape from by lingering deflationary pressures.
•Russia/Ukraine: Escalating tensions could spill over to a wider economic impact if Russian energy exports or international prices are affected. We see the risk of this as low due to Europe’s and Russia’s mutual dependence on energy trade.

B.2 Exchange rates
B.2.1 Trade-weighted dollar drives upwards again
After climbing 6% since the start of the year, the trade weighted dollar has lost some of its strength this month. Underwhelming US data releases have tempered the dollar’s strength but with these Q1 data hurt by the transitory impact of cold weather and west coast port problems, a reversal in fortunes in Q2 and diverging monetary policy will continue to make the dollar relatively attractive. CRU sees the dollar continuing to gain this year but at a lesser pace as the RMB and the euro find support near current levels.
B.2.2 Monetary policy divergence still key for euro
The euro has recovered some ground against the dollar recently, having briefly dropped below 1.05€/$. It is currently trading at around 1.09€/$ (0.91$/€) but volatility is high. The rally followed a Federal Reserve press conference where, despite a change in forward guidance – no longer being ‘patient’, markets focused on Janet Yellen, Chair of the Board of Governors, emphasising that they’re not going to be ‘impatient’. Nonetheless, monetary policy divergence continues to underpin the forecast. Our forecast has been revised this month; we now expect the euro to reach 1.00(€/$) by the end of 2015 and maintain that level, with little evidence that monetary policy will converge, in the medium term.
B.2.3 Balance of risks remain on downside for RMB
As CRU noted previously, the Chinese authorities appear reluctant for the RMB/$ to fall much beyond
6.25.In March the yuan moved in a narrow range between a low of 6.27 and a high of 6.21 later in the month. We expect the RMB to come under renewed downward pressure as Chinese interest rates fall further and US rates rise: we forecast that the currency will quickly return to the lower bound of 6.25, staying there till mid-2016. However, in real effective terms the RMB reached a new high in February according to the Bank for International Settlements, 9.7% above its 2014 average. The balance of risks is for the RMB/$ to be allowed to weaken beyond 6.25 to help exporters that are being squeezed by the weakness of other currencies.
B.2.4 More Bank of Japan stimulus to send yen to ¥130 by year- end
The yen has fallen by more than 12% against the US dollar so far this year compared to 2014. The Bank of Japan’s extension to its asset purchase programme (QQE) at the end of last year (when the annual pace of purchases was raised to ¥80 trillion from ¥60-70 trillion) has weighed on the currency and in March the yen fell to ¥121 to the dollar. We think that the sluggish economy and the central bank’s determination to lift inflation expectations will mean a further increase of QQE this year. We expect this to push the yen to ¥130 by the end of the year.
B.2.5Central bank supports Mexican peso
After falling to the weakest level on record against the US dollar this month, Mexico’s peso found support from the central bank which announced it would hold daily auctions of more than $50 million for the next three months on top of support offered whenever the peso depreciates by more than 1.5% in a day. Weak oil prices and security issues have damaged some of the optimism around the country’s wide- ranging reforms (aimed at improving potential growth in the economy) at a time when the dollar has gone from strength to strength. The peso has seen its slide accentuate recently but CRU expects the peso to appreciate through the year as domestic conditions improve.

B.4 Scenario
B.4.1 Upside: Lower oil - market struggles to find the bottom of the barrel
Crude oil prices have recovered some ground since sinking to $45/bbl in January, but remain soft at $60/bbl. Last year crude was driven up well beyond $100/bbl as market participants took to precautionary buying on the back of expectations of threats to output in the wake of unrest and conflict in MENA. Supply has returned and met with weaker than expected demand (since revised up by energy institutions), causing a surge in inventories, hitting prices.
Permanently lower oil prices are increasing likely, given Saudi Arabia’s change in policy to defend market share rather than prices. In this scenario, we consider the impact of lower prices throughout the forecast. We assume $42/bbl in 2015, before recovering to $70/bbl in 2017 through to 2019. Our assumption implies existing higher cost US shale oil output, with average short run marginal costs of $25- 30/bbl, continues unaffected. However, future production, is threatened. High decline rates mean that 50-70% of a well is drained within a year. Typically, new drilling requires prices around $65/bbl – meaning that in our scenario some output is lost.
Our estimates show that global growth is boosted by 20 to 30bp per year during 2015-17 but falls relative to the base case further out as prices recover. The impact varies by country: while net oil exporters lose out, consumers of oil benefit. China and India stand to gain the most from reduced import bills and improved fiscal balances. Economic growth in both China and India increases by a up to 60bp in 2016. GDP growth in the Eurozone, US and Japan also picks up pace under the scenario. On the other hand, lower oil prices mean a drop in revenue for net oil exporting economies. Russia, reliant on oil and gas for two-thirds of exports, is amongst those most worse off relative to our base case, falling by almost three percentage points more in 2015.
Table B.7: Alternative Scenario – Oil prices stay lower longer (percentage change in real GDP/GNP year-on-year)
Table B.7: Alternative Scenario – Oil prices stay lower longer (percentage change in real GDP/GNP year-on-year) – Concluded.
B.4.2 Downside: China investment crunch
As CRU has repeatedly warned, the balance of risks to the Chinese outlook are to the downside. Post- 2008 China experienced an increase in credit that is enormous by international historical experience as local governments and corporates binged on credit to fund investment. Total Social Financing (TSF) excluding equity – a measure of credit outstanding – ballooned from 115% of GDP at the end of 2008 to 187% at the end of 2014. Experience of other countries is that large and rapid increases in leverage are associated with financial crisis. China is also undertaking structural reforms affecting key elements of the economy, including State Owned Enterprises, local government finances and the financial sector. The reforms are vital for China’s future longer-term economic success, but bring with them implementation risks.
In this scenario the risks come to fruition, causing investment growth to grind to a halt in 2015 (base case 5.9%) due to real estate downturn and local government cutbacks. Industrial output growth slows to 3% (7.5%). The government responds with infrastructure investment, looser fiscal policy, eased housing market restrictions and growth supporting reforms. Having learnt lessons from 2008, however, the response is smaller and slower this time. Investment recovers in 2016, lifting industrial output growth which also benefits from re-stocking. Medium term growth rates return towards the base case, but the level of GDP in 2019 is 3% smaller (equal to US$385bn) than in the base case. The GDP impact is smaller than that seen in other countries that experienced a financial crisis, reflecting : our base case already factors in slowing growth due to the challenges China faces; the low share of foreign currency debt; and the capacity for the government to respond. Our base case GDP forecast for 2015-2019 averages 6.6% p.a. compared to the 8.6% recorded in the five years to 2014 and the 10% trend growth prior to that.
China’s downturn hits other countries via reduced imports, particularly of commodities as both prices and volumes weaken. Investor confidence around the world also suffers, which is particularly damaging to vulnerable emerging market countries.
